Refuting the Claim: The Qur'an Itself Proves Its Uncreatedness
Al-Ash'ari refutes the claim that the Qur'an's uncreatedness is not found in the Qur'an.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari refutes the argument that the uncreatedness of the Qur'an is not mentioned in the Qur'an by citing specific verses and exposing logical inconsistency.

Core Idea
The Qur'an explicitly proves its own uncreatedness through its verses.

Imam al-Ash'ari addresses a key objection from those who deny the uncreatedness of the Qur'an: that this doctrine is not found in the Qur'an itself. He turns the table by quoting verses that distinguish creation from command, the divine word 'Be', and the infinite nature of Allah's words. He then exposes the logical flaw in the opponent's methodology, demanding consistency.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
Read the opening line where Al-Ash'ari challenges the assumption that not finding something in the Qur'an means it is absent.
The opponent says: 'We do not find it in the Qur'an, so it is not there.'
2
Read the Qur'anic proofs
Identify the three verses Al-Ash'ari quotes and note how each one indicates that the Qur'an is uncreated.
Verses: Al-A'raf 54, An-Nahl 40, Al-Kahf 109.
3
Grasp the logical argument
Notice how Al-Ash'ari turns the opponent's methodology against them: if they accept interpretations with proof, they should accept these proofs. Otherwise, they must suspend judgment on all disputes.
This exposes inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم : ولم زعمتم أنكم إذا لم تجدوه في القرآن فليس موجوداً فيه؟
ثم إنا نوجدهم ذلك، ونتلو عليهم الآيات التي احتججنا بها في كتابنا هذا، واستدللنا بها على أن القرآن غير مخلوق، كقوله عز وجل: ﴿أَلَا لَهُ الْخَلْقُ وَالأَمْرُ﴾ [الأعراف:٥٤]، وكقوله: ﴿إِنَّمَا قَوْلُنَا لِشَيْءٍ إِذَا أَرَدْنَاهُ أَن نَقُولَ لَهُ كُن فَيَكُونُ﴾ [النحل:٤٠]، وكقوله: ﴿قُل لَّوْ كَانَ الْبَحْرُ مِدَادًا لِكَلِمَاتِ رَبِّي لَنَفِدَ الْبَحْرُ قَبْلَ أَن تَنفَدَ كَلِمَاتُ رَبِّي﴾ [الكهف:١٠٩]، وسائر ما احتججنا في ذلك من آي القرآن.
ويقال لهم : يلزمكم أن تتوقفوا في كل ما اختلف الناس فيه، ولا تقدموا في ذلك على قول، فإن جاز لكم أن تقولوا ببعض تأويل المسلمين إذا دل على صحتها دليل، فلم لا قلتم إن القرآن غير مخلوق بالحجج التي ذكرناها في كتابنا هذا قبل هذا الموضع؟
It was said to them: And why do you claim that when you do not find it in the Qur'an, it does not exist in it? Then we show them that, and we recite to them the verses which we have argued with in this our book, and by which we have inferred that the Qur'an is uncreated, such as His saying, Mighty and Majestic: 'Surely, to Him belongs creation and the command' [Al-A'raf:54], and His saying: 'Our saying to a thing when We intend it is that We say to it "Be," and it is' [An-Nahl:40], and His saying: 'Say, "If the sea were ink for the words of my Lord, the sea would be exhausted before the words of my Lord are exhausted"' [Al-Kahf:109], and the rest of what we have argued with in that regard from the verses of the Qur'an. And it is said to them: It follows that you must stop in every matter about which people differ, and not express an opinion about it. For if it is permissible for you to adopt some of the interpretations of the Muslims when a proof indicates its correctness, then why do you not say that the Qur'an is uncreated, based on the proofs we have mentioned in this our book before this point?
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who deny the uncreatedness of the Qur'an on the grounds that it is not explicitly stated in the Qur'an. Al-Ash'ari first challenges their assumption: just because they do not find it does not mean it is absent. He then presents concrete verses from the Qur'an that prove the Qur'an's uncreatedness: verse 54 of Surah Al-A'raf (creation and command are distinct), verse 40 of Surah An-Nahl (the divine word 'Be'), and verse 109 of Surah Al-Kahf (the infinite words of God). These verses indicate that the Qur'an (as divine speech) is not part of creation. He then makes a logical argument: if the opponents are willing to accept certain interpretations when proofs are presented, they should accept the proofs for the uncreatedness of the Qur'an. Otherwise, they must suspend judgment on all disputed matters. This exposes their inconsistency.

Misconception: The Qur'an does not mention the uncreatedness of the Qur'an.
Correction: The Qur'an contains verses that logically imply the uncreatedness of divine speech, such as Al-A'raf 54, An-Nahl 40, and Al-Kahf 109.

The Qur'an's Multiple Realities: In Preserved Tablet, Hearts, Recitation, and Writing
Al-Ash'ari affirms the multiple realities of the Qur'an: in the Preserved Tablet, hearts, recitation, and writing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Qur'an, being the eternal speech of Allah, simultaneously exists in multiple forms—in the Preserved Tablet, in the hearts of scholars, recited by tongues, and written in mushafs—all in reality, not metaphorically.

Core Idea
The Qur'an is one speech, yet it is truly in the tablet, hearts, recitations, and writings.

This passage from Al-Ash'ari's al-Ibānah addresses a common question: Is the Qur'an in the Preserved Tablet? Al-Ash'ari answers yes, but then expands the answer, showing that the Qur'an also exists in the hearts of those who know it, is recited by tongues, and is written in our mushafs. He insists that all these are real, not metaphorical. This lesson will help you appreciate how classical Sunni theology understands the oneness and multiplicity of the divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Al-Ash'ari uses Qur'anic evidence for each mode.
2
Identify the four modes of existence
The passage lists four distinct modes: in the Preserved Tablet, in hearts, recited, and written. Find each one and the verse cited.
Also note the emphasis on 'in reality' (في الحقيقة).
3
Understand the unifying principle
Al-Ash'ari does not see these as contradictions. How are they all truly the Qur'an? Consider that it is one eternal speech manifesting in these forms.
This connects to the broader Sunni doctrine of the uncreated Qur'an.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Some might think that being in the Preserved Tablet means it is not recited or written. Al-Ash'ari's answer shows they are compatible.
Think about how this defends against ta'til or tashbih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : حدثونا ، أتقولون إن كلام الله في اللوح المحفوظ؟ قيل له : كذلك نقول ؛ لأن الله عز وجل قال : ﴿بَلْ هُوَ قُرْآنٌ مَجِيدٌ * فِي لَوْحٍ مَحْفُوظٍ﴾ [البروج:٢١-٢٢]، فالقرآن في اللوح المحفوظ. وهو في صدور الذين أوتوا العلم، قال الله عز وجل: ﴿بَلْ هُوَ آيَاتٌ بَيِّنَاتٌ فِي صُدُورِ الَّذِينَ أُوتُوا الْعِلْمَ﴾ [العنكبوت:٤٩]. وهو متلو بالألسنة، قال الله تبارك وتعالى: ﴿لَا تُحَرِّكْ بِهِ لِسَانَكَ لِتَعْجَلَ بِهِ﴾ [القيامة:١٦]. والقرآن مكتوب في مصاحفنا في الحقيقة؛ محفوظ في صدورنا في الحقيقة؛ متلو بألسنتنا في الحقيقة؛ مسموع لنا في الحقيقة؛ كما قال عز وجل : ﴿فَأَجِرْهُ حَتَّى يَسْمَعَ كَلَامَ اللهِ﴾ [التوبة:٦].
If a questioner says: Inform us, do you say that the speech of Allah is in the Preserved Tablet? It is said to him: Thus we say, because Allah, Mighty and Majestic, said: 'Nay, it is a glorious Qur'an, in a Preserved Tablet' [Al-Buruj:21-22]. So the Qur'an is in the Preserved Tablet. And it is in the chests of those who have been given knowledge. Allah, Mighty and Majestic, said: 'Nay, it is clear signs in the chests of those who have been given knowledge' [Al-Ankabut:49]. And it is recited by the tongues. Allah, Blessed and Exalted, said: 'Do not move your tongue with it to make haste with it' [Al-Qiyamah:16]. And the Qur'an is written in our mushafs in reality; preserved in our chests in reality; recited by our tongues in reality; heard by us in reality, as He, Mighty and Majestic, said: 'So grant him asylum so that he hears the speech of Allah' [At-Tawbah:6].
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the various modes in which the Qur'an exists. Al-Ash'ari responds to a hypothetical questioner asking whether the Qur'an is in the Preserved Tablet. He answers affirmatively, citing Surah Al-Buruj. He then expands: the Qur'an is also in the hearts of scholars (Surah Al-Ankabut), recited by tongues (Surah Al-Qiyamah), and written in our mushafs. He emphasizes that all these are 'in reality' (في الحقيقة), not metaphorically. He also mentions that it is heard, citing Surah At-Tawbah. This affirms the doctrine that the Qur'an is a single eternal speech, yet it has multiple manifestations: preserved, memorized, recited, written, and heard. It guards against the idea that these different modes imply multiplicity or change in the divine speech.

Key Terms
اللوح المحفوظ
صدور
تلاوة
مصاحف
حقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'an's being in the Preserved Tablet contradicts its being recited or written; these modes are mutually exclusive.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that the Qur'an truly exists in all these modes simultaneously, as affirmed by multiple Qur'anic verses, without contradiction because it is one speech manifesting in different forms.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an, as Allah's eternal speech, truly exists in the Preserved Tablet, in hearts, in recitation, and in writing, without contradiction.

The Ruling on Uttering the Qur'an (al-Lafz)
Al-Ash'ari forbids saying 'uttered the Qur'an' because it implies creation, affirming only recitation and writing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Ash'ari forbids the phrase 'uttered the Qur'an' and how it relates to the uncreatedness of the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Saying 'uttered the Qur'an' subtly implies the Qur'an is created.

In this passage, Al-Ash'ari addresses a seemingly minor linguistic issue that carries major theological implications. He explains that the phrase 'uttered the Qur'an' is not merely a stylistic choice but a cover for the belief that the Qur'an is created. This lesson unpacks his argument and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Al-Ash'ari begins with a question-and-answer format.
2
Understand the linguistic argument
Al-Ash'ari points out that the Arabic verb 'lafaza' means 'to spit out' or 'throw out', which is inappropriate for the divine speech. Identify this reasoning in the text.
This is the key to the prohibition.
3
Identify the sectarian context
Al-Ash'ari reveals that those who use 'lafz' do so to imply the Qur'an is created. Understand why this is a dangerous innovation.
This connects the linguistic point to the broader theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال : حدثونا عن اللفظ بالقرآن كيف تقولون فيه؟
قيل له : القرآن يقرأ في الحقيقة ويتلى، ولا يجوز أن يقال يلفظ به؛ لأن القائل لا يجوز له أن يقول إنه كلام ملفوظ به؛ لأن العرب إذا قال قائلهم : لفظت باللقمة من في معناه : رميت بها وكلام الله عز وجل لا يقال يلفظ به، وإنما يقال : يقرأ ويتلى ويكتب ويحفظ. وإنما قال قوم: لفظنا بالقرآن ليثبتوا أنه مخلوق، ويزينوا بدعتهم، وقولهم بخلقه، ويدلسوا كفرهم على من لم يقف على معناهم. فلما وقفنا على معناهم أنكرنا قولهم، ولا يجوز أن يقال: إن شيئاً من القرآن مخلوق ؛ لأن القرآن بكامله غير مخلوق.
If he says: Inform us about the utterance (al-lafz) of the Qur'an, what do you say about it? It is said to him: The Qur'an is recited in reality and read, and it is not permissible to say that it is uttered; because the speaker may not say that it is uttered speech; because the Arabs, when one of them says 'I uttered the morsel from my mouth,' its meaning is 'I threw it out' — and the speech of Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is not said to be uttered. Rather, it is said to be recited, read, written, and memorized. And some people said 'We uttered the Qur'an' only to affirm that it is created, and to embellish their innovation, and their statement of its creation, and to disguise their disbelief from those who do not understand their meaning. So when we understood their meaning, we rejected their statement. And it is not permissible to say that anything of the Qur'an is created, because the entire Qur'an is uncreated.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage addresses the controversial issue of 'lafz' (utterance) with respect to the Qur'an. Al-Ash'ari is asked about it. He answers that one should not say 'uttered the Qur'an' because the Arabic verb 'lafaza' means 'to spit out' or 'throw out,' implying casting away. The Qur'an, being divine speech, is not 'thrown out' but rather recited, read, written, and memorized. He then reveals the underlying motive: those who use 'lafz' do so to argue that the Qur'an is created (since their speech is created). By saying 'we uttered the Qur'an,' they subtly imply that the Qur'an itself is a created utterance. Al-Ash'ari therefore forbids this terminology to protect the belief in the uncreatedness of the Qur'an. He concludes by reaffirming that nothing of the Qur'an is created.

Key Terms
اللفظ
ملفوظ
خلق
بدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that saying 'I uttered the Qur'an' is a neutral, acceptable phrase with no theological implications.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that this phrase linguistically implies that the Qur'an is a created thing that is 'thrown out', and it was used by innovators to advance the false doctrine of the creation of the Qur'an.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Ash'ari reject the phrase 'uttered the Qur'an'?
A) It is grammatically incorrect.
B) It was used by those who claim the Qur'an is created.
C) The Qur'an cannot be spoken.
D) It is a bid'ah.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari forbids saying 'uttered the Qur'an' because it linguistically implies creation and was used by innovators to disguise their belief that the Qur'an is created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ash'ari warns that even a single word ('uttered') can disguise disbelief. Are there words or phrases you use when speaking about Islam that might subtly contradict the truth? What will you do to safeguard your speech from today?

The innovators knew exactly what they were doing—they deliberately chose a word to mask their innovation. Have you ever justified a small compromise in your faith by using ambiguous language? Will you repent and correct that today?
?
If someone heard you speak about the Qur'an, would they ever catch a hint of doubt in its uncreatedness? What specific changes will you make to ensure your words reflect the reverence Allah's speech deserves?

Refuting the Objection of the 'Newly Occurring Reminder'
Al-Ash'ari answers an objection by defining 'dhikr muhdath' as prophetic speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari refutes the claim that the phrase 'dhikr muhdath' in the Qur'an implies the Qur'an itself is created, by distinguishing the Qur'an from prophetic reminders.

Core Idea
The 'newly occurring reminder' is not the Qur'an but the Prophet's admonitions.

In defending the uncreatedness of the Qur'an, Al-Ash'ari addresses a key proof text cited by opponents. The verse speaks of a 'newly occurring reminder,' which could be misunderstood as referring to the Qur'an. Al-Ash'ari carefully explains that the reminder here is the Prophet's own speech and admonition, not the eternal Qur'an. This lesson unpacks his concise rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage, including the cited verse and Al-Ash'ari's reply. Notice how he uses another verse to support his interpretation.
The objection appears in the form of a rhetorical question.
2
Distinguish the two meanings of 'dhikr'
Identify how Al-Ash'ari separates the Qur'an from prophetic reminders. He argues that 'dhikr' in the verse is the Prophet's speech, not the Qur'an.
This distinction is crucial for preserving the Qur'an's eternity.
3
Reflect on the argument's logic
Consider why Al-Ash'ari's interpretation is consistent with the overall doctrine. If the 'dhikr' were the Qur'an, it would imply temporal occurrence; his clarification avoids that.
The second verse (Adh-Dhariyat 51:55) supports that 'dhikr' can refer to admonition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن قال قائل : أليس قد قال الله تعالى: ﴿مَا يَأْتِيهِم مِّن ذِكْرٍ مِّن رَّبِّهِم مُحْدَثٍ إِلَّا اسْتَمَعُوهُ وَهُمْ يَلْعَبُونَ﴾ [الأنبياء:٢] .
قيل له : الذكر الذي عناه الله عز وجل ليس هو القرآن بل هو كلام الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ووعظه إياهم، وقد قال الله تعالى لنبيه : ﴿وَذَكِّرْ فَإِنَّ الذِّكْرَىٰ تَنفَعُ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ﴾.
If a questioner says: Did not Allah, Exalted, say: 'No reminder comes to them from their Lord newly occurring except that they listen to it while they play' [Al-Anbiya:2]? It is said to him: The reminder (al-dhikr) that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, meant is not the Qur'an, but rather it is the speech of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and his admonition to them. And Allah, Exalted, said to His Prophet: 'And remind, for indeed the reminder benefits the believers.'
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection. The opponent cites Surah Al-Anbiya verse 2, which speaks of a 'newly occurring reminder' (dhikr muhdath) coming from their Lord. They might argue that this implies the Qur'an itself is 'newly occurring' (hadith), thus created. Al-Ash'ari refutes this by specifying that 'dhikr' here does not refer to the Qur'an but to the Prophet's admonitions and teachings, which are indeed created and occur in time. He supports this by quoting another verse where Allah commands the Prophet to remind. Thus the 'newly occurring reminder' is not the uncreated Qur'an. This preserves the doctrine that the Qur'an is eternal and not subject to temporal occurrence.

Key Terms
ذكر
محدث
ربهم
كلام الرسول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'dhikr muhdath' refers to the Qur'an, implying it is created.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari clarifies that 'dhikr' in the verse means the Prophet's admonition, not the Qur'an itself.

Checkpoint

A) The Qur'an itself.
B) The Prophet's speech and admonition.
C) The Torah.
D) Angels.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari preserves the uncreatedness of the Qur'an by interpreting 'dhikr muhdath' as the Prophet's created reminders.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you listen to a sermon or read a hadith, do you ever intuitively feel that the Prophet's words carry the same weight as the Qur'an? How does distinguishing between created prophetic speech and the uncreated Qur'an affect the reverence you give to each?
?
If you were challenged by someone using Quran 21:2 to claim the Qur'an is created, would you be able to give Al-Ash'ari's answer from memory? What does that say about your readiness to defend the creed?
?
Is there a 'dhikr muhdath' in your own life—some created reminder (like a friend's advice or a lecture) that you are neglecting while seeking spectacular signs? How will you respond to the reminders that come to you through created means?

Refuting the Claim that the Qur'an is Uttered
Al-Ash'ari refutes that the Qur'an is uttered and created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ash'ari rejects the phrase 'uttering the Qur'an' and how it relates to the doctrine that the Qur'an is uncreated.

Core Idea
The Qur'an is read and recited, not 'uttered'—the term implies creation.

Al-Ash'ari addresses a subtle verbal innovation. Some used the word 'utter' (lafẓ) for the Qur'an, which in Arabic means to spit out or discard. He shows this term was chosen deliberately to imply that the Qur'an is created. By exposing their motive, he upholds the orthodox view that the entire Qur'an is uncreated.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice that it begins with a question about 'uttering' the Qur'an, followed by al-Ash'ari's response.
Pay attention to the shift from the objector's phrasing to the author's correction.
2
Understand the Arabic root 'lafẓ'
Al-Ash'ari explains that 'lafaẓa' in Arabic means to spit out or discard. This is why the term is inappropriate for God's speech.
This linguistic point is key to the argument.
3
Identify the heretical motive
The author reveals that those who say 'we uttered the Qur'an' do so to affirm that it is created and to disguise their disbelief.
Once their true intention is known, their statement is rejected.
4
Affirm the conclusion
The passage concludes with the clear statement that nothing of the Qur'an is created; the entire Qur'an is uncreated.
This is the orthodox position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال : حدثونا عن اللفظ بالقرآن كيف تقولون فيه؟ قيل له : القرآن يقرأ في الحقيقة ويتلى، ولا يجوز أن يقال يلفظ به؛ لأن القائل لا يجوز له أن يقول إنه كلام ملفوظ به؛ لأن العرب إذا قال قائلهم : لفظت باللقمة من في معناه : رميت بها وكلام الله عز وجل لا يقال يلفظ به، وإنما يقال : يقرأ ويتلى ويكتب ويحفظ وإنما قال قوم: لفظنا بالقرآن ليثبتوا أنه مخلوق، ويزينوا بدعتهم، وقولهم بخلقه، ويدلسوا كفرهم على من لم يقف على معناهم فلما وقفنا على معناهم أنكرنا قولهم، ولا يجوز أن يقال: إن شيئاً من القرآن مخلوق ؛ لأن القرآن بكامله غير مخلوق.
If he says: Inform us about the utterance (al-lafẓ) of the Qur'an, how do you speak concerning it? It is said to him: The Qur'an is read in reality (ḥaqīqah) and recited, and it is not permissible to say that it is uttered (lafẓ), because the speaker is not permitted to say that it is spoken speech (kalām malfūẓ bihī), because the Arabs, when one of them says 'I spat out (lafaẓtu) the morsel from my mouth,' its meaning is: I threw it away, and the speech of God Almighty is not said to be uttered. Rather it is said: It is read, recited, written, and memorized. And a people said: 'We uttered (lafaẓnā) the Qur'an' only to affirm that it is created, to adorn their innovation (bid'ah) and their statement of its creation, and to disguise their disbelief upon those who did not understand their meaning. So when we understood their meaning, we denounced their statement. And it is not permissible to say that anything of the Qur'an is created, because the entire Qur'an is uncreated (ghayr makhliq).
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (mas'ala). The objector asks how the author (al-Ash'ari) speaks about 'uttering' (al-lafẓ) the Qur'an. The answer explains that the Qur'an is read and recited in reality, but the term 'uttered' is not used because in Arabic, 'lafaẓa' means to spit out or discard, which is inappropriate for God's speech. The author then exposes the heretical motive behind using such language: those who say 'we uttered the Qur'an' intend to imply it is created and to disguise their disbelief (kufr). Once their true meaning is understood, their statement is rejected. The passage concludes by affirming that no part of the Qur'an is created; the whole Qur'an is uncreated (ghayr makhliq).

Key Terms
اللفظ
ملفوظ به
الحقيقة
مخلوق
غير مخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Using the phrase 'uttering the Qur'an' is a neutral way to describe recitation.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that the term 'lafẓ' implies discarding and was intentionally used by heretics to subtly affirm that the Qur'an is created.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Ash'ari reject the phrase 'uttered the Qur'an' (lafaẓnā bil-Qur'ān)?
A) Because the Qur'an is not sound.
B) Because the Arabic root 'lafẓ' means to spit out or discard, implying contempt and creation.
C) Because the Qur'an was revealed in a single letter.
D) Because recitation is the only valid form.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an is not 'uttered' but read and recited; it is entirely uncreated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson reveals that even a single word—'uttered'—can hide a dangerous belief. Examine your own speech: are there any common phrases you use about the Qur'an or Allah that may carry unintended theological implications? What will you change starting today?
?
The innovators disguised their disbelief behind familiar language. In your own life, where might you be accepting a familiar but incorrect description of Islamic beliefs because it sounds harmless? Are you willing to deeply investigate every term you use about Allah and His attributes?

The Meaning of 'Dhikr Muhdath' in Q21:2
Al-Ash'ari refutes an objection from Q21:2 about 'dhikr muhdath'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's refutation of the claim that Q21:2 proves the Qur'an is created, and his identification of the 'dhikr muhdath' as the Prophet's speech.

Core Idea
The 'dhikr muhdath' is the Prophet's speech, not the Qur'an.

In this passage, al-Ash'ari addresses an objection based on Q21:2. The opponent argues that since the verse mentions a 'newly produced reminder' (dhikr muhdath), the Qur'an must be created. Al-Ash'ari responds with two arguments: first, that the dhikr refers to the Prophet's own admonition, not the Qur'an; second, that the verse's wording does not necessitate that every dhikr is muhdath. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the verse
Read the objector's citation of Q21:2. Notice the opponent's logic: they assume 'dhikr' refers to the Qur'an and 'muhdath' means created.
2
Identify al-Ash'ari's first argument
He interprets 'dhikr' as the Prophet's speech and admonition, citing Q65:10-11 where the Prophet is called a 'dhikr' and is created (muhdath).
This shifts the referent from the Qur'an to the Prophet.
3
Identify al-Ash'ari's second argument
He points out that the verse's phrasing 'min dhikrin muhdath' does not assert that every dhikr is muhdath; it is a description, not a restriction. He uses an analogy about a man from Tamīm.
This linguistic point undermines the opponent's inference.
4
Reflect on the significance
Al-Ash'ari's method combines scriptural interpretation and logical analysis to defend the uncreatedness of the Qur'an.
This shows the careful reasoning of Sunni kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن قال قائل : أليس قد قال الله تعالى: (مَا يَأْتِيهُم مِّن ذِكْرٍ مِّن رَّبِّهِم تُحْدَثٍ إِلَّا اسْتَمَعُوهُ وَهُمْ يَلْعَبُونَ) [الأنبياء:٢] . قيل له : الذكر الذي عناه الله عز وجل ليس هو القرآن بل هو كلام الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ووعظه إياهم، وقد قال الله تعالى لنبيه : (وَذَكِّرْ فَإِنَّ الذِّكْرَىٰ تَنفَعُ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ) [الذاريات : ٥٥] وقد قال الله تعالى : (قَدْ أَنزَلَ اللهُ إِلَيْكُمْ ذِكْرًا * رَسُولًا يَتْلُوا عَلَيْكُمْ مَا يَتِ اللَّهِ مبتني) [الطلاق:١٠-١١] فسمى الرسول ذكراً، والرسول محدث . وأيضاً فإن الله عز وجل قال : (مَا يَأْتِيهِم مِّن ذِكْرٍ مِّن رَّبِّهِم تُحْدَثٍ إِلَّا اسْتَمَعُوهُ وَهُمْ يَلْعَبُونَ) يخبر أنه لا يأتيهم ذكر محدث إلا استمعوه وهم يلعبون، ولم يقل لا يأتيهم ذكر إلا كان محدثاً، وإذا لم يقل هذا لم يوجب أن يكون القرآن محدثاً . ولو قال قائل: ما يأتيهم رجل من التميميين يدعوهم إلى الحق إلا أعرضوا عنه، لم يوجب هذا القول أنه لا يأتيهم رجل إلا كان تميمياً، فكذلك القول فيما سألونا عنه .
If a questioner says: Did not God Almighty say: 'No dhikr (reminder) comes to them from their Lord newly produced (muḥdath) except they listen while they play' (Q21:2)? It is said to him: The dhikr that God Almighty meant is not the Qur'an, but rather it is the speech of the Messenger (ṣallā llāhu ʿalayhi wa-sallam) and his admonition of them. And God Almighty said to His Prophet: 'And remind, for the reminder benefits the believers' (Q51:55). And God Almighty said: 'God has sent down to you a dhikr, a Messenger reciting to you the clear signs of God' (Q65:10-11). So He called the Messenger a dhikr, and the Messenger is created (muḥdath). And also, God Almighty said: 'No dhikr comes to them from their Lord newly produced (muḥdath) except they listen while they play.' He informs that no newly produced dhikr comes to them except they listen while they play, and He did not say 'No dhikr comes to them except it is newly produced.' And since He did not say this, it does not necessitate that the Qur'an is created (muḥdath). And if someone were to say: 'No man from the Tamīm tribe comes to them calling them to the truth except they turn away from him,' this statement would not necessitate that no man comes to them except he is a Tamīmī. So likewise is the case concerning what they asked us.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a further objection-and-answer. The objector cites Q21:2 which mentions a 'dhikr muḥdath' (newly produced reminder) and argues that this implies the Qur'an is created. Al-Ash'ari responds in two ways. First, he argues that the 'dhikr' in that verse refers not to the Qur'an but to the Prophet's own speech and admonition, as supported by Q65:10-11 where the Prophet himself is called a 'dhikr' and the Prophet is created (muḥdath). Second, al-Ash'ari makes a linguistic/logical argument: the verse says 'no dhikr comes to them except they listen...' It does not say 'no dhikr comes to them except it is newly produced.' The phrase 'min dhikrin muḥdath' could be descriptive, not restrictive. He uses an analogy: saying 'no man from Tamīm comes to them except they turn away' does not mean every man who comes is from Tamīm. Similarly, the verse does not affirm that every dhikr that comes is muḥdath, so it cannot be used to prove the Qur'an is created.

Key Terms
ذكر
محدث
الرسول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Q21:2 proves the Qur'an is created because the verse mentions a 'newly produced reminder' (dhikr muhdath).
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows the dhikr in that verse refers to the Prophet's speech, not the Qur'an, and the wording does not necessitate that all dhikr is created.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, what does 'dhikr' in Q21:2 refer to?
A) The Qur'an
B) The Torah
C) The speech and admonition of the Prophet Muhammad
D) The angels

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari defends the uncreatedness of the Qur'an by showing that the 'dhikr muhdath' in Q21:2 refers to the Prophet's speech, not the Qur'an, and that the verse's wording does not imply creation of the Qur'an.

Al-Ash'ari uses precise linguistic analysis to protect the uncreatedness of the Qur'an. Are you as careful with the words of Allah when you speak about Him? Do you ever make casual statements about God's attributes without ensuring they align with revelation?

The Qur'an and Iron: Refuting an Analogy
Al-Ash'ari refutes the analogy between the createdness of iron and the Qur'an.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari refutes the analogy between the 'sending down' of iron and the Qur'an to argue for the uncreatedness of the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Sending down does not imply creation; the nature of what is sent down matters.

In this passage, al-Ash'ari addresses a specific objection from those who claim the Qur'an is created because it is described as 'sent down' just like iron. He shows that the analogy fails because iron is a physical body, while the Qur'an is speech, not a body. This lesson examines his precise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the opponents quote Q39:28 and Q57:25 to claim that the Qur'an, like iron, must be created.
Notice they use the same verb 'anzala' (sent down) for both.
2
Identify al-Ash'ari's response
Read al-Ash'ari's reply carefully. He distinguishes iron as a 'jism mawāt' (inanimate body) and argues that the Qur'an is not a body.
The key point: the category of 'sent down' does not entail being a body.
3
Understand the logic
Ask yourself: why does the fact that iron is a body matter? Because if the Qur'an is not a body, then the analogy is invalid.
This refutes the objection without denying that iron is created.
4
Apply the principle
Consider how this argument protects the doctrine of the uncreated Qur'an while acknowledging that God sends down both revelation and physical objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن سألونا عن قول الله عز وجل: (قرآنا عربيا) [الزمر : ٢٨] قيل لهم : الله عز وجل أنزله وليس مخلوقاً . فإن قالوا: فقد قال الله تعالى: (وَأَنزَلْنَا الْحَدِيدَ فِيهِ بَأْسٌ شَدِيدٌ) [الحديد: ٢٥] والحديد مخلوق . قيل لهم : الحديد جسم موات وليس يجب إذا كان القرآن منزلاً، أن يكون جسماً مواتاً، ولذلك لا يجب إذا كان القرآن منزلاً أن يكون مخلوقاً، وإن كان الحديد مخلوقاً .
And if they ask us about the saying of God Almighty: 'An Arabic Qur'an' (Q39:28), it is said to them: God Almighty sent it down and it is not created. And if they say: God Almighty said: 'And We sent down iron, in which is severe might' (Q57:25), and iron is created. It is said to them: Iron is an inanimate body (jism mawāt), and it is not necessary that if the Qur'an is sent down, it should be an inanimate body. Therefore it is not necessary that if the Qur'an is sent down, it should be created, even if iron is created.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer. The objectors argue from Q39:28 that the Qur'an is described as 'sent down' (unzila), and they compare it to iron in Q57:25 which is also 'sent down' and is clearly created. Al-Ash'ari responds by distinguishing the nature of what is sent down: iron is a physical, inanimate body (jism mawāt), whereas the Qur'an is speech, not a body. The category of 'sent down' does not entail materiality or creation. Thus, even though iron is created, the Qur'an remains uncreated because its essence is different. The argument hinges on rejecting a false analogy: inzal (sending down) does not by itself imply that the thing sent down is created; rather, it is the nature of the thing that determines its createdness.

Key Terms
أنزل
جسم موات
مخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'inzal' (sending down) always implies creation.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that 'inzal' applies to both created (iron) and uncreated (Qur'an) things; it is the nature of the thing, not the verb, that determines createdness.

Checkpoint
How does al-Ash'ari refute the analogy that the Qur'an is created because iron is created and both are 'sent down'?
A) He denies that iron is sent down.
B) He argues that the Qur'an is not sent down but revealed.
C) He distinguishes iron as a dead body (jism mawāt) whereas the Qur'an is not a body.
D) He says the Qur'an was sent down to the earth in a different way.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari refutes the analogy by distinguishing between a physical body (iron) and non-physical speech (Qur'an), showing that 'sending down' does not entail createdness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If someone today argued that the Qur'an is created because it was 'sent down' like iron, could you immediately point out the flaw in that reasoning? Have you made al-Ash'ari's distinction your own?
?
How does the uncreatedness of the Qur'an affect your relationship with it? Does it compel you to listen, obey, and reflect with the urgency it deserves, or have you become comfortable and negligent? Reflect on your last interaction with the Qur'an.

Proof from Isti'adha: Allah's Speech is Uncreated
Imam al-Ash'ari argues from the command to seek refuge to the uncreatedness of Allah's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the command to seek refuge in Allah's words logically proves that His speech is uncreated.

Core Idea
Seeking refuge in created things is forbidden; seeking refuge in Allah's speech is commanded, so it must be uncreated.

Imam al-Ash'ari presents a concise yet powerful argument from the Islamic practice of seeking refuge (isti'adha). The argument hinges on a simple observation: we are commanded to seek refuge in Allah's perfect words, but we are never commanded to seek refuge in any created thing. This leads to the necessary conclusion that Allah's speech is not created. The passage directly refutes the Mu'tazilite position that the Qur'an is created.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: the argument has three clear parts.
The Arabic uses 'فقد وجب' (then it is necessarily established) to mark the conclusion.
2
Identify the two premises
The argument builds on two premises: (1) We are commanded to seek refuge in Allah (uncreated) and in His perfect words. (2) We are never commanded to seek refuge in any created thing.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
Since seeking refuge in Allah's speech is commanded, and no created thing is sought refuge in, Allah's speech cannot be created. This is a deductive proof.
The reasoning is similar to a syllogism: All X are Y; this is X; therefore this is Y.
4
Reflect on the refutation of the Mu'tazilites
This argument directly counters the claim that the Qur'an is created. If the Qur'an were created, we would be commanded to seek refuge in a created thing, which contradicts the second premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جواب : ويقال لهم : قد أمرنا الله عز وجل أن نستعيذ به وهو غير مخلوق، وأمر أن نستعيذ بكلمات الله التامات، وإذا لم نؤمر أن نستعيذ بمخلوق من المخلوقات وأمرنا أن نستعيذ بكلام الله فقد وجب أن كلام الله غير مخلوق.
Answer: It is said to them: Verily Allah Almighty commanded us to seek refuge in Him, and He is uncreated, and He commanded us to seek refuge in the perfect words of Allah. And since we are not commanded to seek refuge in any created thing among created things, and we are commanded to seek refuge in the speech of Allah, then it is necessarily established that the speech of Allah is uncreated.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a proof from scripture for the uncreatedness of Allah's speech (kalam). The argument proceeds from the established command to seek refuge (isti'adha). The author states that Allah commanded us to seek refuge in Himself, who is uncreated, and also to seek refuge in His perfect words (kalamat Allah al-tammat). Since we are never commanded to seek refuge in any created thing, and we are commanded to seek refuge in Allah's speech, it follows necessarily that His speech is not created. The logic is: (1) All commands to seek refuge are either in the Creator (uncreated) or in creation. (2) We are commanded to seek refuge in Allah's speech. (3) We are not commanded to seek refuge in any created thing. Therefore, Allah's speech must be uncreated. This refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that the Quran is created.

Key Terms
استعاذة (isti'adha)
كلام الله (kalam Allah)
غير مخلوق (ghayr makhluq)
كلمات الله التامات (kalamat Allah al-tammat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeking refuge in words implies they are created because words are sounds or letters that come into existence.
Correction: The author argues the opposite: the command to seek refuge in them proves they are divine and uncreated, because we are never commanded to seek refuge in anything created.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the command to seek refuge in Allah's words prove they are uncreated?
A) Because we are commanded to seek refuge in Allah Himself, who is uncreated, and in His words, so they must share His attribute of uncreatedness.
B) Because we are forbidden from seeking refuge in anything created.
C) Because the words are eternal like Allah.
D) Because the Prophet (PBUH) said so.

Key Takeaway
The command to seek refuge in Allah's speech, combined with the prohibition of seeking refuge in created things, proves that Allah's speech is uncreated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If seeking refuge in created things is forbidden, where do you instinctively run when you are terrified? Does your immediate reaction align with trusting Allah's uncreated words? What needs to change?
?
The command to seek refuge in Allah's speech is not optional—it is a divine summons. In your daily life, which 'created refuges' (people, comforts, distractions) do you rely on more than the words of Allah?
?
Imagine standing on the Day of Judgment and being asked: 'Did you truly believe that My speech alone is uncreated and sufficient?' What evidence would your life give of that belief? Commit to one specific change starting today.

The Doctrine of Allah's Istiwa on the Throne
Imam al-Ash'ari explains Allah's istiwa on the Throne using Quranic verses and supplication practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Ash'ari's doctrine that Allah establishes Himself on the Throne in a manner befitting Him, negating physical settling, and the scriptural and practical evidence for this.

Core Idea
Allah's istiwa is affirmed without modality, as the Qur'an and Muslim practice show.

This passage from Al-Ibanah presents the classical Ash'ari position on Allah's istiwa. Imam al-Ash'ari responds to a question by affirming the attribute while rejecting any anthropomorphic interpretation. He then marshals multiple Qur'anic verses and the universal practice of raising hands in supplication to demonstrate that Allah is above His Throne, yet without being confined or physically resting.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the author begins with a question-and-answer format.
2
Identify the key attributes
Note the two-fold affirmation: istiwa is affirmed, but with the caveat 'ghayr tawl al-istiqrar' (without settling). This is the Ash'ari via media.
3
Examine the scriptural evidence
The author cites six verses. List them and see how each supports the doctrine of Allah's aboveness.
4
Understand the explanation of 'man fi al-sama'
The author clarifies that 'He who is in the heaven' means on the Throne above the heavens, not indwelling.
5
Reflect on the practical proof
Consider the universal practice of raising hands in supplication. Why does this serve as evidence? It shows Muslims instinctively recognize Allah's aboveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن قال قائل : ما تقولون في الاستواء؟ قيل له : نقول : إن الله عز وجل يستوي على عرشه استواء يليق به من غير طول الاستقرار كما قال الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى [طه:٥] وقد قال الله عز وجل: إِلَيْهِ يَصْعَدُ الْكَلِمُ الطَّيِّبُ [فاطر:١٠] وقال: بَل رَفَعَهُ اللَّهُ إِلَيْهِ [النساء:١٥٨]، وقال عز وجل: يُدَيرُ الأَمرَ مِنَ السَّمَاءِ إِلَى الْأَرْضِ ثُمَّ يَعْرُجُ إِلَيْهِ [السجدة:٥]، وقال تعالى حكاية عن فرعون: هَامَانُ ابْنِ لِي صَرْحًا لَعَلَى أَبْلُغُ الأَسْبَابَ أَسْبَابَ السَّمَوَاتِ فَأَطَّلِعَ إِلَى إِلَهِ مُوسَى وَإِنِّي لَأَظُنُّهُ كَذِبًا [غافر:٣٦-٣٧] فكذب فرعون موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام في قوله : إن الله عز وجل فوق السموات. وقال عز وجل: أَمِنتُم مَّن فِي السَّمَاءِ أَن يَخْسِفَ بِكُمُ الْأَرْضَ [الملك:١٦]. فالسموات فوقها العرش، فلما كان العرش فوق السموات قال: أَمِنْتُم مَّن فِي السَّمَاءِ لأنه مستو على العرش الذي فوق السموات، وكل ما علا فهو سماء، فالعرش أعلى السموات، وليس إذا قال: أَمِنتُم مَّن فِي السَّمَاءِ يعني جميع السموات، وإنما أراد العرش الذي هو أعلى السموات، ألا ترى أن الله عز وجل ذكر السموات، فقال تعالى: وَجَعَلَ الْقَمَرَ فِيهِنَّ نُورًا [نوح:١٦] ولم يرد أن القمر يملأهن جميعاً، وأنه فيهن جميعاً، ورأينا المسلمين جميعاً يرفعون أيديهم إذا دعوا نحو السماء؛ لأن الله عز وجل مستو على العرش الذي هو فوق السموات، فلولا أن الله عز وجل على العرش لم يرفعوا أيديهم نحو العرش، كما لا يحطونها إذا دعوا إلى الأرض.
If a questioner says: What do you say about al-istiwa? It is said to him: We say: Verily Allah Almighty establishes Himself (yastawi) on His Throne in a manner befitting Him, without [the meaning of] settling or resting, as the Most Merciful said: 'The Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne' [Taha:5]. And Allah Almighty said: 'To Him ascends the good word' [Fatir:10], and He said: 'Rather, Allah raised him to Himself' [An-Nisa:158], and He Almighty said: 'He directs the command from the heaven to the earth; then it ascends to Him' [As-Sajda:5]. And He Almighty said, relating from Pharaoh: 'O Haman, build for me a tower that I may reach the means, the means of the heavens, and look upon the God of Moses; and indeed, I think he is a liar' [Ghafir:36-37]. So Pharaoh denied Moses, peace be upon him, in his statement that Allah Almighty is above the heavens. And He Almighty said: 'Do you feel secure that He who is in the heaven will not cause the earth to swallow you?' [Al-Mulk:16]. So the heavens, above them is the Throne. Since the Throne is above the heavens, He said: 'Do you feel secure that He who is in the heaven' because He is established (mustawin) on the Throne which is above the heavens. And everything that is elevated is called heaven, so the Throne is the highest of the heavens. It is not that when He said 'He who is in the heaven' He meant all the heavens; rather, He intended the Throne which is the highest of the heavens. Do you not see that Allah Almighty mentioned the heavens, saying: 'And He set the moon therein as a light' [Nuh:16] and He did not intend that the moon fills them all, and that it is in them all? And we see all Muslims raising their hands when they supplicate towards the heaven, because Allah Almighty is established on the Throne which is above the heavens. If Allah Almighty were not on the Throne, they would not raise their hands towards the Throne, just as they do not lower them when supplicating towards the earth.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal exposition on the attribute of istiwa (establishing) as affirmed by the Qur'an and the consensus of Muslims. The author begins by presenting a question about istiwa and then answers by affirming that Allah establishes Himself on His Throne in a manner befitting Him (yaliq bihi), explicitly negating the interpretation of physical settling or resting (ghayr tawl al-istiqrar). He then cites multiple Qur'anic verses to support the doctrine that Allah is above His creation, specifically above the Throne which is above the heavens. The verses cited include: (1) Taha:5, the primary verse on istiwa. (2) Fatir:10, which speaks of ascent of good words to Him. (3) An-Nisa:158, about raising Jesus to Him. (4) As-Sajda:5, about the ascent of affairs to Him. (5) Ghafir:36-37, where Pharaoh denies Moses' claim that Allah is above the heavens. (6) Al-Mulk:16, which says 'He who is in the heaven'. The author then explains that 'in the heaven' refers to the Throne, not that Allah is contained within the heavens. He uses the moon in the heavens as an analogy: the moon is in the heavens but does not fill them, similarly Allah is above the Throne without being confined. Finally, he cites the universal practice of Muslims raising hands towards heaven in supplication, demonstrating that they recognize Allah's aboveness. The argument is that this practice would be meaningless if Allah were not above. This passage defends the classical Ash'ari position that Allah is above His Throne without modality (bila kayf), rejecting both corporealist and Mu'tazilite interpretations.

Key Terms
الاستواء
العرش
استواء يليق به
غير طول الاستقرار
من في السماء
رفع الأيدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Istiwa means Allah sits on the throne in a physical sense or like a creature.
Correction: Imam al-Ash'ari affirms istiwa but immediately negates any notion of settling (tawl al-istiqrar), indicating it is an attribute befitting Allah without modality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT cited as evidence for Allah's istiwa?
A) 'The Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne' (Taha:5)
B) 'To Him ascends the good word' (Fatir:10)
C) 'He who is in the heaven' (Al-Mulk:16)
D) 'Say: He is Allah, the One' (Al-Ikhlas:1)

Key Takeaway
Allah's istiwa on the Throne is a true attribute affirmed by the Qur'an and Muslim practice, understood without physical localization or resemblance to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you raise your hands in du'a, do you truly perceive that you are addressing a Lord who is above all, or has it become a mere routine? What would change if you paused to reflect on the meaning of 'Allah is above the Throne' before every supplication?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah's istiwa in a physical way, as if He were sitting? How does the phrase 'without settling or resting' confront such thoughts, and what can you do to purify your heart of them?
?
If the Muslim practice of raising hands is a witness to Allah's aboveness, does your prayer life bear that witness? When you prostrate, do you feel the humility of being before a Lord who is transcendent yet near? What step can you take today to make your salah more conscious of His majesty?

The True Meaning of Allah's Istiwa upon the Throne
Imam Ash'ari defines Allah's istiwa and proves it with Quran and practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct meaning of Allah's istiwa upon the Throne as taught by Imam Ash'ari, supported by Quranic verses and the practice of Muslims.

Core Idea
Allah's istiwa is real and befitting His majesty, not a metaphor.

In this passage from al-Ibanah, Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari directly addresses the question of what Muslims should believe about Allah's istiwa (establishment) upon the Throne. He provides a clear definition, multiple Quranic proofs that Allah is above the heavens, and a powerful practical proof from the universal practice of Muslims raising their hands towards the sky in supplication. This lesson unpacks these evidences to establish the correct belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Let the author's own words and flow sink in before analyzing.
2
Identify the definition of istiwa
Find the part where the author states what istiwa means. Notice he says it is 'without any manner of resting' (bi ghayri ṭūl al-istiqrār).
This refutes the idea that istiwa involves physical contact or change.
3
Examine the Quranic proofs
The author quotes several verses. List them and see how each supports that Allah is above the heavens and upon the Throne.
The verses include 20:5, 35:10, 4:158, 32:5, 40:36-37, and 67:16.
4
Understand the practical proof from Muslim practice
Note the argument that all Muslims raise their hands towards the sky when supplicating, which indicates they believe Allah is above, upon the Throne.
This is a consensus-based proof that the belief is implicit in the community.

If someone says: What do you say about al-istiwa (the being established)? It is said to him: We say: Indeed Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is established (yastawi) upon His Throne in a manner befitting Him, without any [manner of] resting, as He said: 'The Most Merciful is established upon the Throne' (20:5). And Allah, Mighty and Majestic, said: 'To Him ascend the good words' (35:10), and He said: 'Rather, Allah raised him to Himself' (4:158), and He, Mighty and Majestic, said: 'He arranges the matter from the heaven to the earth; then it ascends to Him' (32:5), and He, Exalted, related from Pharaoh: 'O Haman, build for me a tower that I might reach the means, the means of the heavens, and look at the God of Moses; and indeed, I think he is a liar' (40:36-37). So Pharaoh belied Moses, the Prophet of Allah, peace and blessing be upon him, in his saying that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is above the heavens. And He, Mighty and Majestic, said: 'Do you feel secure that He who is in the heaven will not cause the earth to swallow you?' (67:16). So the heavens are above them the Throne. So when the Throne was above the heavens, He said: 'Do you feel secure that He who is in the heaven?' because He is established (mustawin) upon the Throne which is above the heavens, and everything high is heaven, so the Throne is the highest of the heavens. And when He said: 'Do you feel secure that He who is in the heaven?' He did not mean all the heavens, but rather He meant the Throne which is the highest of the heavens. Do you not see that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, mentioned the heavens and said: 'And He made the moon therein a light' (71:16), and He did not mean that the moon fills them all and that it is in them all. And we see all the Muslims raising their hands when they supplicate towards the heaven, because Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is established upon the Throne which is above the heavens. So if Allah, Mighty and Majestic, were not upon the Throne, they would not raise their hands towards the Throne, just as they do not lower them when they supplicate to the earth.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the true meaning of istiwa (Allah's being established) upon the Throne. The author begins by presenting a hypothetical question: 'What do you say about al-istiwa?' The answer states that Allah is established upon His Throne in a manner befitting Him, without any form of physical resting or contact. This definition is immediately supported by quoting the Quranic verse 'The Most Merciful is established upon the Throne' (20:5). Then the author adduces multiple Quranic verses that indicate Allah's highness and the fact that things ascend to Him: 'To Him ascend the good words' (35:10), 'Rather, Allah raised him to Himself' (4:158), 'Then it ascends to Him' (32:5). The narrative of Pharaoh is cited, where Pharaoh sought to ascend to the heaven to see the God of Moses, thereby confirming that Moses taught that Allah is above the heavens. Then the verse 'Do you feel secure that He who is in the heaven...' (67:16) is interpreted: 'He who is in the heaven' refers to Allah being upon the Throne which is above the heavens, because 'heaven' in this context means everything high, and the Throne is the highest. The author clarifies that 'in the heaven' does not mean containment within the heavens, just as in 'He made the moon therein a light' (71:16) does not mean the moon fills all the heavens. Finally, a practical proof from the consensus of Muslims: they raise their hands towards the sky when supplicating, directed to the Throne, and they do not lower them to the earth. This practice is based on the belief that Allah is upon His Throne above the heavens.

Correction: Imam Ash'ari shows istiwa is real and proven by Quran and the unanimous practice of Muslims, though without modality (how).

Refuting the Interpretation of Istiwa as Domination
Imam Ash'ari refutes the interpretation of istiwa as domination via reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why interpreting 'istiwa' as 'domination' leads to an absurd conclusion and must be rejected.

Core Idea
If 'istiwa' meant 'domination,' Allah would be 'established' on all things, including filth.

Imam Ash'ari presents a powerful logical argument against those who reinterpret the Quranic description of Allah's being established upon the Throne. He shows that reducing 'istiwa' to a general attribute of domination leads to unacceptable consequences that no Muslim would affirm.

Guided Path
1
Understand the deviant claim
Read the passage to identify the claim of the Mu'tazila, Jahmiyya, and Haruriyya that 'istiwa' means 'istila' (domination) and that Allah is everywhere.
They deny Allah's specific elevation above the Throne.
2
Trace the logical consequence
Follow the author's argument: if istiwa = istila, then since Allah dominates all things, He would be 'established' on all things equally.
This includes the earth, heavens, and even filthy places.
3
Recognize the absurdity
Note that no Muslim would say Allah is 'established' on lavatories or filth, so the interpretation is invalid.
Thus istiwa must have a specific meaning unique to the Throne.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : وقد قال قائلون من المعتزلة والجهمية والحرورية: إن قول الله عز وجل الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى ﴾ [طه:٥] أنه استولى وملك وقهر ، وأن الله عز وجل في كل مكان وجحدوا أن يكون الله عز وجل على عرشه - كما قال أهل الحق - وذهبوا في الاستواء إلى القدرة . ولو كان هذا كما ذكروه كان لا فرق بين العرش والأرض السابعة؛ لأن الله تعالى قادر على كل شيء، والأرض الله سبحانه قادر عليها، وعلى الحشوش وعلى كل ما في العالم، فلو كان الله مستوياً على العرش بمعنى الاستيلاء، وهو عز وجل مستو على الأشياء كلها لكان مستوياً على العرش وعلى الأرض وعلى السماء وعلى الحشوش والأقذار ؛ لأنه قادر على الأشياء مسئول عليها، وإذا كان قادراً على الأشياء كلها ولم يجز عند أحد من المسلمين أن يقول: إن الله عز وجل مستو على الحشوش والأخلية، تعالى الله عن ذلك علواً كبيراً، لم يجز أن يكون الاستواء على العرش الاستيلاء الذي هو عام في الأشياء كلها، ووجب أن يكون معنى الاستواء يختص بالعرش دون الأشياء كلها
Chapter: And some of the Mu'tazila, Jahmiyya, and Haruriyya have said that the saying of Allah, Mighty and Majestic, 'The Most Merciful is established upon the Throne' (20:5) means He dominated, possessed, and subdued, and that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is in every place, and they denied that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is upon His Throne - as the people of truth say - and they interpreted al-istiwa as power. But if this were as they mentioned, there would be no difference between the Throne and the seventh earth; because Allah, Exalted, is powerful over everything, and Allah - exalted is He - is powerful over the earth, and over lavatories, and over everything in the world. So if Allah were established (mustawiyan) upon the Throne in the sense of domination, and He, Mighty and Majestic, is established over all things, then He would be established upon the Throne and upon the earth and upon the heaven and upon lavatories and filth; because He is powerful over things and has control over them. And if He is powerful over all things, and it is not permissible for any Muslim to say that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is established (mustawin) upon lavatories and cesspits - exalted is Allah above that greatly - then it is not permissible for istiwa upon the Throne to be domination which is general over all things, and it is necessary that the meaning of istiwa be specific to the Throne apart from all other things.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the interpretation that istiwa (being established) means istila (domination, possession, or conquest). The author first states the position of the deviant sects - Mu'tazila, Jahmiyya, and Haruriyya - who claim that 'The Most Merciful is established upon the Throne' means He dominated and possessed, and that Allah is in every place, denying that He is specifically upon His Throne. Then the author provides a logical reductio ad absurdum: If istiwa meant domination (istila), then since Allah dominates all things (He is powerful over everything), that would imply He is 'established' upon the Throne, the earth, the heavens, and even lavatories and filth, because He dominates all equally. However, no Muslim would ever say that Allah is 'established' upon lavatories or filth - such attribution is entirely inappropriate and blasphemous. Therefore, the meaning of istiwa cannot be a general domination (istila) that applies to all created things. Instead, istiwa must have a specific meaning that applies only to the Throne, which is the unique attribute of Allah being elevated upon the Throne in a manner befitting His majesty. This argument shows that the deviant interpretation leads to an absurd consequence, so it must be rejected.

Key Terms
الاستيلاء (al-istila)
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila)
الجهمية (al-Jahmiyya)
الحرورية (al-Haruriyya)
القدرة (al-qudra)
استوى (istawa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Interpreting istiwa as domination is an acceptable theological position.
Correction: This leads to the absurd consequence that Allah would be 'established' upon filth, which no Muslim can accept. Hence the interpretation is false.

Checkpoint
What logical consequence does Imam Ash'ari derive from the interpretation of istiwa as domination (istila)?
A) That the Throne would be lower than the earth
B) That Allah would be established on all things equally, including filth
C) That the Throne would be a physical location
D) That Allah would not be able to control the earth

Key Takeaway
The meaning of 'istiwa' is specific to the Throne and cannot be reduced to general domination, as that leads to an absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's might, do you ever unconsciously reduce His majesty to a mere abstract power that doesn't differ between a throne and a cesspool? How does this passage correct that negligence in your heart?
?
How often do you accept a comfortable interpretation of Allah's attributes just because it avoids uncomfortable literal reading, without realizing it leads to greater blasphemy? What will you do now to embrace what Allah says about Himself, even when your intellect rebels?
?
If we cannot attribute istiwa to Allah as He deserves, what does that say about your own willingness to submit to Allah's speech over your own reasoning? Where else in your life have you reshaped divine truth to fit your comfort?

4 min
Refuting the Interpretation of Istiwa' as Domination
Imam Ash'ari refutes interpreting istiwa' as istila by showing it leads to no distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why interpreting Allah's istiwa' as general domination (istila) leads to an absurd conclusion that no Muslim accepts, and why the true meaning must be specific to the Throne.

Core Idea
If istiwa' meant domination, Allah would 'dominate' filth – an impossibility.

Imam Ash'ari presents a powerful logical refutation of those who interpret 'istiwa' as mere domination. He shows that if it were so, it would apply equally to all things, including the most degrading places. Since no Muslim would ever say that, the interpretation must be false, and istiwa' must have a unique meaning reserved for the Throne.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing view
Read the passage and note what the Mu'tazila and others claim about istiwa'.
They say it means domination, possession, and that Allah is everywhere.
2
Follow the reductio argument
Notice how Imam Ash'ari takes their premise and applies it universally to show its absurd consequences.
If istiwa' means domination, then it applies to all things equally, including filth.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Understand that the only way to avoid absurdity is to accept that istiwa' has a special meaning specific to the Throne.
This argument defends the orthodox belief that Allah is above His Throne in a manner befitting His majesty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قال قائلون من المعتزلة والجهمية والحرورية: إن قول الله عز وجل الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى أنه استولى وملك وقهر ، وأن الله عز وجل في كل مكان وجحدوا أن يكون الله عز وجل على عرشه - كما قال أهل الحق - وذهبوا في الاستواء إلى القدرة . ولو كان هذا كما ذكروه كان لا فرق بين العرش والأرض السابعة؛ لأن الله تعالى قادر على كل شيء، والأرض الله سبحانه قادر عليها، وعلى الحشوش وعلى كل ما في العالم، فلو كان الله مستوياً على العرش بمعنى الاستيلاء، وهو عز وجل مستو على الأشياء كلها لكان مستوياً على العرش وعلى الأرض وعلى السماء وعلى الحشوش والأقذار ؛ لأنه قادر على الأشياء مسئول عليها، وإذا كان قادراً على الأشياء كلها ولم يجز عند أحد من المسلمين أن يقول: إن الله عز وجل مستو على الحشوش والأخلية، تعالى الله عن ذلك علواً كبيراً، لم يجز أن يكون الاستواء على العرش الاستيلاء الذي هو عام في الأشياء كلها، ووجب أن يكون معنى الاستواء يختص بالعرش دون الأشياء كلها.
And some of the Mu'tazila, Jahmiyya, and Haruriyya have said: That the saying of Allah Almighty 'The Most Merciful upon the Throne istawa' means that He dominated, possessed, and subdued, and that Allah Almighty is in every place, and they denied that Allah Almighty is upon His Throne – as the people of truth say – and they went in (interpreting) istiwa' to (mean) power. And if this were as they mentioned, there would be no difference between the Throne and the seventh earth; because Allah Almighty is powerful over everything, and the earth, Allah glorified is powerful over it, and over latrines and over everything in the world. So if Allah were istawa upon the Throne in the sense of domination (istila), and He, glorified, is istawa upon all things, He would be istawa upon the Throne and upon the earth and upon the heaven and upon latrines and filth; because He is powerful over things and responsible for them. And if He is powerful over all things, and it is not permissible according to any of the Muslims to say that Allah Almighty is istawa upon latrines and privies – exalted is Allah above that with great exaltation – then it is not permissible that istiwa' upon the Throne be the domination that is general over all things, and it is necessary that the meaning of istiwa' be specific to the Throne and not to all things.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila, Jahmiyya, and Haruriyya who interpret 'istiwa' upon the Throne' as meaning 'domination' (istila) and claim that Allah is in every place. Imam Ash'ari begins by stating their position: they say that Allah's istiwa' means He dominated, possessed, and subdued, and that He is everywhere, denying that He is specifically above His Throne. He then proceeds to refute them by showing the logical consequence of their interpretation. If istiwa' meant domination (istila), then it would apply equally to all things because Allah has power over everything. So He would be 'istawa' upon the Throne, upon the earth, upon the heavens, upon latrines, and upon filth, since power is universal. But no Muslim would ever say that Allah is 'istawa' upon filth and privies – that is clearly impossible and an exaltation of Allah above such things. Therefore, the interpretation of istiwa' as general domination is false. Instead, istiwa' must have a meaning that is specific to the Throne and not shared by all other things. This argument uses a reductio ad absurdum: the opponents' premise leads to an unacceptable conclusion, so the premise must be rejected.

Key Terms
الاستواء (istiwa')
الاستيلاء (istila')
العرش ('arsh)
القدرة (qudra)
المعتزلة (Mu'tazila)
الجهمية (Jahmiyya)
الحرورية (Haruriyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'istiwa' means Allah dominates everything in a general sense.
Correction: This passage shows that such a belief leads to the absurd claim that Allah 'dominates' filth, which no Muslim accepts. Thus, istiwa' must have a unique meaning specific to the Throne.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Ash'ari argue that istiwa' cannot mean general domination (istila)?
A) Because the Throne is too high for domination
B) Because domination is specific to the Throne only
C) Because if it meant general domination, it would apply to latrines as well, which no Muslim accepts
D) Because the Mu'tazila themselves rejected that meaning

Key Takeaway
Istiwa' is a unique attribute of Allah related to His Throne, not a general domination shared with all things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the verse 'The Most Merciful upon the Throne istawa,' does your heart accept it without reinterpretation, or do you feel the need to rationalize it? Ask Allah to grant you submission to His revelation.
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were defending tawhid by interpreting istiwa' as domination, but their logic led to absurdity. In what areas of your faith might you be over-rationalizing at the cost of clear texts? Repent and seek the middle path of the Salaf.
?
If someone argued that 'istiwa' can mean domination to avoid anthropomorphism,' how would you use the specific argument from this passage to refute them? Commit to memorizing this logical refutation so you can defend the true attribute of Allah.

Refuting the Claim that Allah is Everywhere
Imam Ash'ari refutes the claim that Allah is everywhere by showing it leads to impossible consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the belief that Allah is essentially in every place leads to impossible contradictions, and how Imam Ash'ari uses logical reasoning to defend Allah's transcendence.

Core Idea
Allah cannot be in every place, or else He would be in filthy places—an impossibility.

Imam Ash'ari confronts a claim from the Mu'tazila, Haruriyya, and Jahmiyya that Allah is in every place. He shows that this leads to both theological absurdity (being in the womb of Mary and latrines) and logical contradiction (being both above and below). This lesson walks through his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Imam Ash'ari uses a very direct and confrontational tone.
2
Identify the opponents' claim
The passage begins by naming three groups: Mu'tazila, Haruriyya, and Jahmiyya. They claim that Allah is in every place. Mark this claim.
These groups are known for extreme views on divine attributes.
3
Notice the first absurdity
Imam Ash'ari immediately points out an absurd consequence: if Allah is in every place, then He is in the womb of Mary and in latrines. This is clearly contrary to religion.
This is a reductio ad absurdum—showing that the claim leads to something obviously false.
4
Follow the logical response
The second part is a detailed response (jawaab). It assumes the opponents' position and shows it leads to contradiction: Allah would have to be under the earth yet above it, above what is beneath Him and beneath what is above Him.
The logic is: if Allah is in every place, He is in the earth, so He is under the earth. But the earth is above Him, etc. This yields a contradiction.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by declaring such a view impossible and contradictory, and exalts Allah above the opponents' fabrication.
The key takeaway is that Allah's transcendence is preserved by logical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعمت المعتزلة والحرورية والجهمية أن الله عز وجل في كل مكان فلزمهم أنه في بطن مريم وفي الحشوش والأخلية، وهذا خلاف الدين، تعالى الله عن قولهم علواً كبيراً. جواب : ويقال لهم : إذا لم يكن مستوياً على العرش بمعنى يختص العرش دون غيره كما قال ذلك أهل العلم ونقلة الآثار وحملة الأخبار، وكان الله عز وجل في كل مكان، فهو تحت الأرض التي السماء فوقها، وإذا كان تحت الأرض والأرض فوقه، والسماء فوق الأرض، ففي هذا ما يلزمكم أن تقولوا : إن الله تحت التحت والأشياء فوقه، وأنه فوق الفوق والأشياء تحته، وفي هذا ما يجب أنه تحت ما هو فوقه وفوق ما هو تحته، وهذا هو المحال المتناقض ، تعالى الله عن افترائكم عليه علواً كبيراً .
And the Mu'tazila, Haruriyya, and Jahmiyya claimed that Allah Almighty is in every place, so it is necessary for them that He is in the womb of Mary and in latrines and privies, and this is contrary to religion, exalted is Allah above their saying with great exaltation. Response: And it is said to them: If He is not istawa upon the Throne in a meaning specific to the Throne without other than it, as the people of knowledge, transmitters of traditions, and bearers of reports said, and Allah Almighty is in every place, then He is under the earth which the heaven is above it, and if He is under the earth while the earth is above Him, and the heaven is above the earth, then in this is what necessitates you to say that Allah is under the beneath while things are above Him, and that He is above the above while things are beneath Him, and in this is what necessitates that He is beneath what is above Him and above what is beneath Him, and this is impossible and contradictory, exalted is Allah above your fabrication against Him with great exaltation.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that Allah is in every place, made by the Mu'tazila, Haruriyya, and Jahmiyya. Imam Ash'ari first states their claim and immediately points out an absurd consequence: if Allah is in every place, then He is in the womb of Mary and in latrines and privies, which is contrary to religion and an impossibility for Allah. Then he provides a more detailed response (jawaab). The response builds on the previous argument that istiwa' is specific to the Throne. If Allah is not specifically upon His Throne and is instead in every place, then He would be under the earth (since the earth is a place), with the earth above Him and the heaven above the earth. This leads to contradictory statements: that Allah is under what is above Him and above what is beneath Him – a logical impossibility. The argument shows that the opponents' position leads to self-contradiction, which is a reductio ad absurdum. The conclusion is that such a view is a fabrication against Allah.

Key Terms
في كل مكان (in every place)
الحشوش (latrines)
الأخلية (privies)
تحت (under)
فوق (above)
المحال (impossible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'everywhere' means Allah's knowledge or power, not His essence. But the opponents here claim He is physically/essentially in every place.
Correction: The passage specifically refutes the claim of essential presence, not just knowledge or power. The absurdities (womb, latrines, above/below) only apply if presence is essential.

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence does Imam Ash'ari derive from the claim that Allah is in every place?
A) That Allah would be above the heavens
B) That Allah would be in the womb of Mary and in filthy places
C) That Allah would not be able to hear prayers
D) That Allah would be confined to the Throne

Key Takeaway
The claim that Allah is in every place leads to logical and religious impossibilities, proving the necessity of His transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought of Allah as 'everywhere' without considering the implications? How does this passage force you to reexamine your mental image of where Allah is?
?
If you truly believed that Allah is above His Throne and distinct from creation, how would that change the way you approach Him in prayer? Would you feel more awe and reverence?
?
The opponents' claim leads to logical absurdity. Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah that, if thought through, might lead to contradictions? Will you examine them in light of this proof?
