Allah's Hands Are Not Favors: The Conclusion
Concludes that 'with My two hands' does not mean favors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse 'with My two hands' cannot mean 'My two favors' and how the argument from Adam's virtue over Iblis proves literal hands.

Core Idea
If 'hands' meant 'favors,' Adam would have no excellence over Iblis—but he does.

In this concluding passage from the proof that Allah's hands are real attributes, Imam al-Ash'ari draws the logical conclusion. He shows that interpreting 'hands' as 'favors' would collapse the distinction between Adam and Iblis, contradicting the Qur'anic argument. Let's see how the conclusion ties the proof together.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the conditional logic: if hands = favors, then Adam has no virtue.
2
Understand the logical structure
Identify the implication: if 'with My two hands' meant 'My two favors', then Adam would have no special virtue over Iblis. But the Qur'an shows Adam does have virtue, so the interpretation must be wrong.
3
Appreciate the conclusion
Realise that the only consistent meaning is that Allah has real hands, not favors. This is a decisive refutation of metaphorical interpretations.
This conclusion relies on the earlier proof that the verse indicates Adam's virtue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوجب أنه لا فضيلة لآدم عليه السلام على إبليس في ذلك، وإنما احتج على إبليس بذلك ليريه أن لآدم عليه السلام في ذلك فضيلة. فدل ما قلناه على أن الله عز وجل لما قال : لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَيَّ لم يعن نعمتي .
It necessitates that Adam, peace be upon him, has no virtue over Iblis in that matter, and rather He argued against Iblis thereby to show him that Adam, peace be upon him, has virtue in that. So what we have said indicates that when Allah, Mighty and Majestic, said: 'for what I created with My two hands' He did not mean 'My two favors'.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a concluding statement from a preceding proof. The author argues that the verse 'for what I created with My two hands' (Saad 38:75) must refer to literal hands, not favors. If it meant favors, then Adam would have no virtue over Iblis, since both were created by favors. But the Qur'an shows that Adam has virtue over Iblis, as God argued against Iblis using this verse. Therefore, 'yadayya' means two hands, not two favors.

Key Terms
يد
نعمة
آدم
إبليس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'yadayya' (My two hands) in the verse means 'two favors' (ni'matay).
Correction: The passage proves that if it meant favors, Adam would have no virtue over Iblis, but the Qur'an shows he does, so it must mean literal hands.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why does 'with My two hands' not mean 'My two favors'?
A) Because favors are not hands.
B) Because if it meant favors, Adam would have no virtue over Iblis.
C) Because Iblis also has favors.
D) Because the verse is metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'with My two hands' must refer to literal hands, not favors, because otherwise Adam would have no excellence over Iblis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that Allah created you with His own hands, how would your view of yourself change? What one lie about yourself would you need to reject today because of that truth?
?
The lesson shows that Adam's superiority is tied to his direct creation. You too are a direct creation of Allah. When you face someone who looks down on you, or when you compare yourself to others, will you ground your identity in being created by Allah's hands? How can you remind yourself of this daily?
?
Allah argued against Iblis by pointing to Adam's special creation. Is there an area of your life where you are ashamed of how Allah made you—your past, your weaknesses, your circumstances? Will you instead see them as part of Allah's personal workmanship and let that silence the accuser in your heart?

Objection and Answer on Allah's Hands
Refutes the claim that Allah's hands must be limbs if not favors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of analogy from the witnessed to the unseen (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid) is applied correctly to defend Allah's attributes without likening Him to creation.

Core Idea
Analogy to creation must be rejected when it implies God is like created beings.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari responds to an objection that if Allah's 'hands' do not mean 'favors,' they must mean physical limbs. He demonstrates that such reasoning is based on a flawed analogy from the created world. By carefully applying the principle of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid, he shows that we can affirm hands for Allah that are unlike any created hands, just as we affirm His existence and attributes without likeness.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the back-and-forth structure of the argument.
The passage is a complete objection-and-answer unit.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent claims that 'hand' in Arabic, if not meaning favor, must mean a physical limb (jarhah). Understand why this is problematic.
3
Examine the author's method of refutation
The author uses two counter-analogies: (1) If we analogize from creation, we would have to say God is a body of flesh and blood, which is false. (2) If we accept that God is a living being unlike us, we should also accept His hands unlike ours. Observe how he turns the opponent's own logic against them.
This demonstrates the correct application of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم : لم أنكرتم أن يكون الله عز وجل عنى بقوله : ( يَدَيَّ ) يدين ليستا نعمتين؟ فإن قالوا: لأن اليد إذا لم تكن نعمة لم تكن إلا جارحة . قيل لهم : ولم قضيتم أن اليد إذا لم تكن نعمة لم تكن إلا جارحة؟ فإن رجعونا إلى شاهدنا أو إلى ما نجده فيما بيننا من الخلق، فقالوا اليد إذا لم تكن نعمة في الشاهد لم تكن إلا جارحة . قيل لهم : إن حملتم على الشاهد وقضيتم به على الله عز وجل، فكذلك لم نجد حياً من الخلق إلا جسماً لحماً ودماً فاقضوا بذلك على الله عز وجل - تعالى عن ذلك - وإلا كنتم لقولكم تاركين ولاعتلالكم ناقضين، وإن أثبتم حياً لا كالأحياء منا، فلم أنكرتم أن تكون اليدان اللتان أخبر الله عز وجل عنهما يدين ليستا نعمتين، ولا جارحتين، ولا كالأيدي؟ وكذلك يقال لهم : لم تجدوا مدبراً حكيماً إلا إنساناً، ثم أثبتم أن للدنيا مدبراً حكيماً ليس كالإنسان، وخالفتم الشاهد ونقضتم اعتلالكم فلا تمنعوا من إثبات يدين ليستا نعمتين ولا جارحتين من أجل أن ذلك خلاف الشاهد
And it is said to them: Why did you deny that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, meant by His saying 'My two hands' two hands that are not two favors? For if they say: Because the hand, if it is not a favor, is nothing but a limb. It is said to them: And why did you judge that the hand, if it is not a favor, is nothing but a limb? If they refer us to our witnessed world or to what we find among us of creation, and they say: The hand in the witnessed world, if it is not a favor, is nothing but a limb. It is said to them: If you apply the witnessed world and judge by it upon Allah, Mighty and Majestic, then likewise we do not find any living being among creation except as a body of flesh and blood, so judge that upon Allah, Mighty and Majestic—exalted is He above that—or else you would be abandoning your own statement and contradicting your reasoning. And if you affirm a living being unlike the living beings from us, then why do you deny that the two hands which Allah, Mighty and Majestic, informed about are two hands that are not favors, nor limbs, nor like [other] hands? And likewise it is said to them: You do not find a wise disposer except a human, then you affirm that the world has a wise disposer not like a human, and you contradict the witnessed world and nullify your reasoning. So do not prevent affirming two hands that are not favors nor limbs because that is contrary to the witnessed world.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (mas'alah) about the nature of Allah's hands. The opponent argues that if 'yad' does not mean 'favor', it must mean 'limb' (jarhah). The author refutes by using the principle of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogy from the unseen to the witnessed). He points out that if we analogize from creation, we would also have to say God is a body of flesh and blood, which is absurd. Instead, when we affirm a living being unlike any created being, we should also affirm hands unlike any created hands. The author also uses an analogy from affirming a wise disposer for the world: though in the witnessed world all wise disposers are human, we affirm God as a wise disposer not like humans. Similarly, we affirm hands not like created hands.

Key Terms
يد
جارحة
شاهد
غائب
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Imam al-Ash'ari rejects all analogical reasoning from creation when discussing Allah's attributes.
Correction: He does not reject analogy altogether; he only rejects faulty analogies that impose creaturely limitations on Allah. He uses a proper analogy that affirms attributes without likeness.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the claim that a hand must be a limb if it is not a favor?
A) He agrees but says the limb is unlike others.
B) He argues that the same analogy would make God a body of flesh and blood, which is false.
C) He says the verse is metaphorical.
D) He says hands are favors after all.

Key Takeaway
The proper method of analogy from the created world does not force us to attribute creaturely limitations to Allah; rather, it affirms His attributes without likeness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read this passage, do you find yourself tempted to think of Allah's hands in terms of human hands? Where in your heart do you still feel the need to compare Allah to something you know? This lesson challenges you to let go of that crutch. Are you willing to affirm Allah's hands as real but utterly unlike anything you can imagine, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
The opponent in this passage is trying to limit Allah's attributes based on their own experience. In what areas of your life—your understanding of Allah's mercy, His justice, His decree—are you still relying on your own finite perspective rather than submitting to what Allah has told you? What would it take for you to let your limited worldview be shattered?
?
The Imam's argument here is not just academic; it's a call to humble your intellect before revelation. When you face a verse that seems difficult, do you trust that Allah's words are true and that your understanding is limited, or do you try to force the verse to fit your expectations? This lesson demands that you choose: will you submit to the text, or will you be like the opponent who rejects what he cannot grasp?

Interpreting 'Aydin' Through Consensus
Objection-and-answer on interpreting 'aydin'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how consensus (ijma') guides the interpretation of the plural 'aydin' in the Qur'an to affirm two hands for Allah.

Core Idea
Consensus determines that 'aydin' means two hands, not many.

This lesson addresses an objection: if we affirm two hands from one verse, why not many from another? The answer lies in the role of consensus in interpreting ambiguous terms, showing how the Qur'an's apparent meaning is preserved even when shifted.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opponent's question carefully: Why do you affirm two hands from 'yadayya' but not many hands from 'aydina'? Notice the apparent contradiction being raised.
The opponent assumes both plurals should be treated the same way.
2
Grasp the answer based on consensus
The author points to consensus (ijma') that affirming many hands is invalid. Reflect on why consensus is a valid basis for interpretation here.
Consensus does not override the text; it helps determine the intended meaning.
3
See how the apparent meaning is shifted
The author explains that 'aydin' is interpreted back to two hands because consensus provides evidence to move from one apparent meaning to another. Understand that the Qur'an remains on its apparent meaning unless evidence requires a shift.
The shift is not arbitrary; it is to another apparent meaning that is a reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا إذا أثبتم الله يدين لقوله : لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَيَّ فلم لا أثبتم له أيدي لقوله: ﴿ مِمَّا عَمِلَتْ أَيْدِينَا ﴾ [يس: ۷۱] ؟ قيل لهم : قد أجمعوا على بطلان قول من أثبت الله أيدي، فلما أجمعوا على بطلان قول من قال ذلك وجب أن يكون الله عز وجل ذكر أيدي، ورجع إلى إثبات يَدَيْن؛ لأن الدليل عنده دل على صحة الإجماع ، وإذا كان الإجماع صحيحاً وجب أن يرجع من قوله : أيد إلى يَدَيْن؛ لأن القرآن على ظاهره ، ولا يزول عن ظاهره إلا بحجة، فوجدنا حجة أزلنا بها ذكر الأيدي عن الظاهر إلى ظاهر آخر، ووجب أن يكون الظاهر الآخر على حقيقة لا يزول عنها إلا بحجة .
For if they say: When you affirm two hands for Allah because of His saying 'with My two hands', why do you not affirm many hands for Him because of His saying 'from what Our hands have made'? It is said to them: They have agreed upon the invalidity of the statement of whoever affirmed many hands for Allah. So, when they agreed upon the invalidity of the statement of whoever said that, it became necessary that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, mentioned 'aydin' (hands) and it refers back to affirming two hands; because evidence with Him indicated the soundness of consensus. And when consensus is sound, it is necessary that His saying 'aydin' be interpreted back to 'two hands'; because the Qur'an is upon its apparent meaning, and it is not removed from its apparent meaning except by evidence. So we found evidence by which we removed the mention of 'aydin' from the apparent meaning to another apparent meaning, and it became necessary that the other apparent meaning be based on reality which is not removed except by evidence.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a second objection-and-answer. The opponent asks: if you affirm two hands from 'yadayya', why not affirm many hands from 'aydina'? The answer relies on consensus (ijma'). The author states that there is consensus that affirming many hands for Allah is false. Therefore, the plural 'aydin' in the verse must be interpreted back to the meaning of two hands (the minimum plural), because the Qur'an is taken in its apparent meaning unless evidence requires otherwise. Here, consensus provides that evidence, so 'aydin' is understood as two hands. Thus, the apparent meaning of 'aydin' is not abandoned entirely; it is shifted to another apparent meaning (two hands) which is a reality that cannot be removed without evidence.

Key Terms
أيدي
إجماع
ظاهر
يدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think consensus can override a clear Qur'anic text arbitrarily.
Correction: Consensus does not override the text; it guides the interpretation of an ambiguous term to another valid apparent meaning, preserving the Qur'an's authority.

Checkpoint
Why does the author interpret 'aydin' (أيدي) as two hands rather than many hands?
A) Because the word 'aydin' always means two in Arabic.
B) Because consensus (ijma') rejects many hands and supports two hands.
C) Because the verse explicitly says 'two hands'.
D) Because the opponent's objection is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Consensus provides the evidence to interpret the plural 'aydin' as two hands, preserving the Qur'an's apparent meaning while rejecting an invalid attribution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a Qur'anic verse that seems to imply something about Allah's attributes, do you immediately assume your first understanding is correct, or do you check it against the consensus of the scholars? What stops you from doing the latter?
?
This lesson shows that consensus can overrule a grammatical implication. Are you willing to submit your personal interpretations to the collective understanding of the early scholars, even when it feels counterintuitive?
?
If you were to teach someone about Allah's hands, would you emphasize the literal affirmation of two hands, or the role of consensus in shaping that belief? Which approach anchors your own faith more firmly?

Consensus Determines the Meaning of 'Aydin'
Rejects the possibility that 'aydin' means one hand using consensus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how consensus (ijma') is used to determine the correct interpretation of a verse, specifically that the plural 'aydin' refers to two hands, not one or many.

Core Idea
Consensus rules out extremes, leaving the apparent meaning intact.

This passage presents an objection: if the word 'aydin' (hands) can mean two, why not one? The answer relies on consensus (ijma') to reject both many and one, then affirms the apparent meaning (zahir) of the text. This shows how consensus functions as a decisive proof in Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: why can't 'aydin' mean one hand if it can mean two? This is a genuine challenge based on Arabic usage.
2
See how consensus eliminates extremes
The response invokes consensus (ijma') that both 'many hands' and 'one hand' are invalid. This leaves only two hands as a possibility.
Consensus here acts as a decisive proof that precludes certain interpretations.
3
Grasp the principle of apparent meaning
The Qur'an is understood according to its apparent meaning (zahir) unless a clear proof indicates otherwise. Consensus is such a proof, but it only removes extremes, not the zahir.
This principle is foundational in Sunni hermeneutics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : إذا ذكر الله عز وجل الأيدي وأراد يَدَيْن، فما أنكرتم أن يذكر الأيدي ويريد يداً واحدة؟ قيل له : ذكر الله عز وجل أيد وأراد يَدَيْن؛ لأنهم أجمعوا على بطلان قول من قال: أيد كثيرة، وقول من قال: يداً واحدة، فقلنا: يدان؛ لأن القرآن على ظاهره، إلا أن تقوم حجة بأن يكون على خلاف الظاهر .
For if a sayer says: When Allah, Mighty and Majestic, mentions 'al-aydi' and intends two hands, why do you deny that He mentions 'al-aydi' and intends one hand? It is said to him: Allah, Mighty and Majestic, mentioned 'ayd' and intended two hands; because they have agreed upon the invalidity of the statement of whoever said: many hands, and the statement of whoever said: one hand. So we said: two hands; because the Qur'an is upon its apparent meaning, unless an evidence arises that it is contrary to the apparent meaning.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a third objection-and-answer. The opponent suggests that perhaps 'aydin' (hands) could mean a single hand, since Arabic sometimes uses the plural for a singular. The author responds that there is consensus (ijma') that both the interpretation of many hands and the interpretation of one hand are invalid. Therefore, the only remaining possibility is two hands. This is because the Qur'an is understood in its apparent meaning (zahir) unless evidence indicates otherwise. Here, consensus provides that evidence for excluding many and one, leaving two as the only valid interpretation. The principle is that the apparent meaning is upheld unless a clear proof shifts it, and consensus is such a proof.

Key Terms
أيدي
يد واحدة
إجماع
ظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that consensus on what is not allowed is enough, but the author uses it to positively determine the meaning of the text.
Correction: Consensus does not just negate false interpretations; it also helps identify the correct one by eliminating options, leaving the apparent meaning as the only valid choice.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the interpretation that 'aydin' means one hand?
A) Because the word 'aydin' cannot mean one hand.
B) Because there is consensus that one hand is invalid.
C) Because the verse clearly says two.
D) Because the context requires a different meaning.

Key Takeaway
The author uses consensus to eliminate both the interpretation of many hands and one hand, leaving two hands as the valid interpretation, upholding the apparent meaning of the Qur'an.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Qur'an, do you 'force' it to fit what you already believe, or do you let its apparent meaning guide you, even when it challenges your assumptions? What evidence from this passage would you use to correct yourself?
?
The author uses consensus to eliminate extreme interpretations. In your own life, are there any 'extreme' views about Allah's attributes that you have held, and are you willing to submit to the consensus of the scholars? How would you practically align yourself with the correct understanding?
?
The principle is that the Qur'an is upon its apparent meaning unless evidence proves otherwise. Are there verses about Allah's attributes that you have reinterpreted without clear evidence? What would it mean for you to accept them as they appear?

Why Not Many Hands? — Consensus Limits the Plural
Defense of two hands using consensus to interpret plural 'aydi'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ashari uses consensus (ijma) to interpret the plural 'aydi' as referring to exactly two hands.

Core Idea
Consensus is the key that unlocks the intended meaning of ambiguous plurals in the Quran.

The first objection claims that if the verse 'with My two hands' proves two hands, then the verse 'from what Our hands have made' (using the plural 'aydi') should prove many hands. Al-Ashari responds by invoking a powerful tool: the consensus of the early community that affirming many hands is false. This consensus forces the plural to be understood as the minimum number, which is two. Let’s examine how this reasoning unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its reasoning
Read the first paragraph of the Arabic passage (Mas'ala 1) and its translation. Notice how the opponent tries to extend the logic from two hands to many hands.
The opponent uses a qiyas (analogy) argument.
2
Identify Al-Ashari's answer
Find the point where he says 'they have unanimously agreed on the invalidity of affirming many hands.' Understand that this consensus is the evidence that shifts the apparent meaning.
Consensus (ijma) is considered a binding proof in Sunni theology.
3
Trace the logical flow
Follow the logic: Quran is on its apparent meaning→ consensus proves many hands false→ so 'aydi' must revert to 'yadayn' (two hands)→ the new apparent meaning is real and cannot be changed without evidence.
4
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself: Why does consensus have the power to override a literal plural? How does this protect the doctrine of two hands?
The principle is that consensus can clarify but not contradict the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة :
فإن قالوا إذا أثبتم الله يدين لقوله : لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَيَّ ) فلم لا أثبتم لـه أيدي لقوله: ﴿ مِمَّا عَمِلَتْ أَيْدِينَا ﴾ [يس: ۷۱] ؟
قيل لهم : قد أجمعوا على بطلان قول من أثبت الله أيدي، فلما أجمعوا على بطلان قول من قال ذلك وجب أن يكون الله عز وجل ذكر أيدي، ورجع إلى إثبات يَدَيْن؛ لأن الدليل عنده دل على صحة الإجماع (۱) ، وإذا كان الإجماع صحيحاً وجب أن يرجع من قوله : أيد إلى يَدَيْن؛ لأن القرآن على ظاهره ، ولا يزول عن ظاهره إلا بحجة، فوجدنا حجة أزلنا بها ذكر الأيدي عن الظاهر إلى ظاهر آخر، ووجب أن يكون الظاهر الآخر على حقيقة لا يزول عنها إلا بحجة .

١٦٢ - مسألة :
فإن قال قائل : إذا ذكر الله عز وجل الأيدي وأراد يَدَيْن، فما أنكرتم أن يذكر الأيدي ويريد يداً واحدة؟
قيل له : ذكر الله عز وجل أيد وأراد يَدَيْن؛ لأنهم أجمعوا على بطلان قول من قال: أيد كثيرة، وقول من قال: يداً واحدة، فقلنا: يـدان؛ لأن القرآن على ظاهره، إلا أن تقوم حجة بأن يكون على خلاف الظاهر .

١٦٣ - مسألة :
فإن قال قائل: ما أنكرتم أن يكون قوله : مِمَّا عَمِلَتْ أَيْدِينَا ﴾ [يس: ٧١] وقوله تعالى : لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَيَّ ) على المجاز ؟
قيل له : حُكْمُ كلام الله عز وجل أن يكون على ظاهره وحقيقته، ولا يُخرج الشيء عن ظاهره إلى المجاز إلا بحجة ألا ترون أنه إذا كان ظاهر الكلام العموم، فإذا ورد بلفظ العموم والمراد به الخصوص، فليس هو على حقيقة الظاهر، وليس يجوز أن يعدل بما ظاهره العموم عن العموم بغیر حجة كذلك قول الله عز وجل : لِمَا خَلَقَتُ بِيَدَيَّ ) على ظاهره وحقيقته من إثبات اليدين ولا يجوز أن يعدل به عن ظاهر اليدين إلى مـا ادعاه خصومنا إلا بحجة، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز لمدع أن يدعي أن ما ظاهره العموم فهو على الخصوص، وما ظاهره الخصوص فهو على العموم بغـيـر حجة، وإذا لم يجز هذا لمدعيه بغير برهان لم يجز لكـم مـا ادعيتموه أنـه مجاز بغير حجة، بل واجب أن يكون قوله : لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَيَّ ) إثبات يَدَيْن الله تعالى في الحقيقة غير نعمتين، إذا كانت النعمتان لا يجوز عند أهل اللسان أن يقول قائلهم : فعلت بيدي، وهو يعني النعمتين
Issue: If they say: When you affirm for Allah two hands due to His saying 'for what I created with My two hands', why do you not affirm for Him hands (aydi) due to His saying 'from what Our hands (aydina) have made' [Ya Sin: 71]? It is said to them: They have unanimously agreed on the invalidity of the saying of whoever affirmed hands (aydi) for Allah. Since they unanimously agreed on the invalidity of that saying, it is necessary that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, mentioned 'hands' (aydi) and it reverts to the affirmation of two hands (yadayn); because the evidence in His view indicates the validity of consensus, and when consensus is valid it is necessary that His saying 'hands' (aydi) reverts to 'two hands' (yadayn); because the Qur'an is upon its apparent meaning, and it is not removed from its apparent meaning except by evidence. We found evidence by which we removed the mention of 'hands' (aydi) from the apparent meaning to another apparent meaning, and it is necessary that the other apparent meaning be upon reality, not removed from it except by evidence.

162 - Issue: If someone says: If Allah, Mighty and Majestic, mentioned 'hands' (aydi) and intended two hands (yadayn), what prevents you that He mentioned 'hands' (aydi) and intended a single hand? It is said to him: Allah, Mighty and Majestic, mentioned 'hands' (aydi) and intended two hands; because they unanimously agreed on the invalidity of the saying of whoever said many hands, and the saying of whoever said a single hand. So we say: two hands; because the Qur'an is upon its apparent meaning, unless evidence arises that it is contrary to the apparent meaning.

163 - Issue: If someone says: What prevents you that His saying 'from what Our hands (aydina) have made' [Ya Sin: 71] and His saying 'for what I created with My two hands' are metaphorical? It is said to him: The ruling of Allah's speech is that it is upon its apparent meaning and its reality, and a thing is not removed from its apparent meaning to metaphor except by evidence. Do you not see that if the apparent meaning of speech is generality, and it comes in the wording of generality while the intended meaning is specificity, then it is not upon the reality of the apparent meaning, and it is not permissible to turn what is apparently general away from generality without evidence. Similarly, Allah's saying 'for what I created with My two hands' is upon its apparent meaning and its reality of affirming two hands, and it is not permissible to turn it from the apparent meaning of two hands to what our opponents claim except by evidence. If that were permissible, it would be permissible for a claimant to claim that what is apparently general is specific, and what is apparently specific is general without evidence. And if this is not permissible for its claimant without proof, it is not permissible for you what you claim, that it is metaphor, without evidence. Rather, it is obligatory that His saying 'for what I created with My two hands' affirms two hands of Allah, Most High, in reality, not two blessings, since two blessings – it is not permissible, according to the people of language, for one of them to say 'I did it with my two hands' while meaning the two blessings.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents three objections and their answers defending the affirmation of two literal hands for Allah. The first objection argues: if the proof for two hands is the verse 'with My two hands', why not also affirm multiple hands based on the verse 'from what Our hands have made', where 'aydina' is plural? The answer relies on consensus: the community has agreed that affirming many hands is false. Therefore, the plural 'aydi' must refer to two hands, because the Qur'an is interpreted according to its apparent meaning (zahir) unless evidence requires departure. Consensus serves as that evidence, shifting the apparent meaning from 'multiple hands' to 'two hands', but the new apparent meaning is also real and cannot be changed without further evidence.

The second objection asks: if you interpret 'aydi' as two hands, why not interpret it as a single hand? The answer again invokes consensus: the community has agreed that both a single hand and many hands are invalid. Therefore, the only remaining possibility is two hands, maintaining the apparent meaning unless evidence to the contrary exists.

The third objection proposes that these verses might be metaphorical (majaz). The answer establishes a principle: Allah's speech is to be taken in its literal, real sense (haqiqah). Departure from the literal meaning to metaphor requires evidence. The argument uses an analogy: just as a general (aam) wording cannot be made specific (khaas) without evidence, so too the literal meaning of 'two hands' cannot be abandoned for a metaphorical interpretation without proof. If such metaphorical interpretations were allowed without evidence, then any text could be arbitrarily reinterpreted. Therefore, the verse must be taken literally as affirming two real hands, not two blessings (as some claimed). Al-Ashari emphasizes that language speakers do not say 'I did it with my two hands' while meaning two blessings; thus the literal meaning is the only valid one.

Key Terms
يدان
أيدي
ظاهر
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the plural 'aydi' in the Quran proves that Allah has multiple hands (e.g., three or more).
Correction: Al-Ashari shows that consensus (ijma) prohibits affirming many hands, so the plural must refer to exactly two hands.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence Al-Ashari uses to interpret the plural 'aydi' as two hands?
A) The literal meaning of the word 'yadayn' elsewhere
B) Consensus that many hands are invalid
C) Analogical reasoning from human hands
D) The context of the verse in Surah Ya Sin

Key Takeaway
Consensus (ijma) can clarify the intended meaning of ambiguous Quranic expressions, but it never contradicts the literal text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse whose apparent meaning conflicts with what you were taught, do you seek out the consensus of scholars or trust your own understanding? How does this lesson expose your reliance on individual reasoning over communal scholarship? What would it take to submit to the collective guidance even when it challenges your logic?
?
The passage says consensus shifts the apparent meaning but the new meaning is a reality not to be changed without evidence. Have you ever clung to a personal interpretation of Allah’s attributes simply because it felt logical, while ignoring the established consensus? What is the cost of that pride?
?
If the Quran is not removed from its apparent meaning except by evidence, what evidence are you using to understand Allah’s attributes? Are you applying the same rigorous method as the scholars, or are you following cultural assumptions or personal preference? Will you commit to verifying your beliefs against the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah?

Why Not One Hand? — Consensus Eliminates Extremes
Refutation of the claim that Allah has only one hand

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ashari uses consensus to reject both the claims of one hand and many hands, leaving only two hands as the correct interpretation.

Core Idea
When extremes are refuted by consensus, the middle ground becomes the only valid option.

A second objection builds on the first: if the plural 'aydi' can be interpreted as two hands, why not interpret it as a single hand? Al-Ashari answers by showing that consensus has already eliminated both extremes—many hands and one hand—leaving only the middle option of two hands. This lesson explores how consensus acts as a sieve, filtering out false interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
Read the Arabic passage for Mas'ala 2 and its translation. Notice the opponent's question: if you can reinterpret the plural as two, why not as one?
This is a logical extension of the previous objection.
2
Identify Al-Ashari's dual consensus
Find where he says they agreed on the invalidity of many hands AND the invalidity of one hand. This is key.
The consensus covers both extremes.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since both extremes are invalid, the only valid interpretation is two hands. This is an example of the principle of the Quran remaining on its apparent meaning unless evidence dictates otherwise.
The evidence here is the consensus that eliminates the other possibilities.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider why Al-Ashari uses consensus rather than linguistic analysis alone. What does this tell us about the role of communal agreement in theological method?

Commentary
This passage presents three objections and their answers defending the affirmation of two literal hands for Allah. The first objection argues: if the proof for two hands is the verse 'with My two hands', why not also affirm multiple hands based on the verse 'from what Our hands have made', where 'aydina' is plural? The answer relies on consensus: the community has agreed that affirming many hands is false. Therefore, the plural 'aydi' must refer to two hands, because the Qur'an is interpreted according to its apparent meaning (zahir) unless evidence requires departure. Consensus serves as that evidence, shifting the apparent meaning from 'multiple hands' to 'two hands', but the new apparent meaning is also real and cannot be changed without further evidence.

The second objection asks: if you interpret 'aydi' as two hands, why not interpret it as a single hand? The answer again invokes consensus: the community has agreed that both a single hand and many hands are invalid. Therefore, the only remaining possibility is two hands, maintaining the apparent meaning unless evidence to the contrary exists.

The third objection proposes that these verses might be metaphorical (majaz). The answer establishes a principle: Allah's speech is to be taken in its literal, real sense (haqiqah). Departure from the literal meaning to metaphor requires evidence. The argument uses an analogy: just as a general (aam) wording cannot be made specific (khaas) without evidence, so too the literal meaning of 'two hands' cannot be abandoned for a metaphorical interpretation without proof. If such metaphorical interpretations were allowed without evidence, then any text could be arbitrarily reinterpreted. Therefore, the verse must be taken literally as affirming two real hands, not two blessings (as some claimed). Al-Ashari emphasizes that language speakers do not say 'I did it with my two hands' while meaning two blessings; thus the literal meaning is the only valid one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself leaning toward an extreme view about Allah's attributes (either denying them or exaggerating them), are you willing to submit to the consensus of the scholars and take the middle path, even if it feels less dramatic or less intellectual? What is blocking you from that submission?
?
The passage uses consensus to eliminate false interpretations. In your own life, where might you be holding onto a belief or practice that contradicts the clear consensus of the Salaf, because it feels more comfortable or more profound? Are you ready to let it go?
?
The principle 'the Quran is upon its apparent meaning unless evidence necessitates otherwise' keeps interpretation from running wild. Reflect on a time you interpreted a Quranic verse based on your own desires rather than the guidance of the scholars. How can you humbly return to the apparent meaning today?

Why Not Metaphor? — The Principle of Literal Interpretation
Argument that divine attributes are literal unless proven metaphorical

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ashari's argument that Allah's speech must be interpreted literally (haqiqah) unless there is clear evidence to interpret it metaphorically.

Core Idea
Metaphor is the exception, not the rule—and the exception requires proof.

The third objection cuts to the heart of the matter: why not simply say that the verses about Allah's hands are metaphorical? Al-Ashari responds by establishing a fundamental hermeneutical principle: Allah's speech is to be taken in its literal, real sense (haqiqah). To abandon the literal meaning for metaphor (majaz) requires compelling evidence. Without such evidence, the literal meaning stands. This lesson unpacks that argument, including the powerful analogy about general and specific language.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection and answer
Read the full Arabic passage for Mas'ala 3 and its translation. Pay attention to the structure: objection, principle, analogy, conclusion.
This is the longest and most detailed answer.
2
Identify the core principle
Find Al-Ashari's statement: 'The ruling of Allah's speech is that it is upon its apparent meaning and its reality.' This is the foundation.
Haqiqah (reality) is the default, not majaz (metaphor).
3
Analyze the analogy
He compares the situation to general (aam) and specific (khaas) wording. Just as you cannot make a general statement specific without evidence, you cannot make a literal statement metaphorical without evidence.
The analogy reinforces the burden of proof on those claiming metaphor.
4
Consider the consequence
If metaphorical interpretation were allowed without evidence, anyone could reinterpret any text arbitrarily. This preserves the integrity of divine revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة :
فإن قالوا إذا أثبتم الله يدين لقوله : لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَيَّ ) فلم لا أثبتم لـه أيدي لقوله: ﴿ مِمَّا عَمِلَتْ أَيْدِينَا ﴾ [يس: ۷۱] ؟
قيل لهم : قد أجمعوا على بطلان قول من أثبت الله أيدي، فلما أجمعوا على بطلان قول من قال ذلك وجب أن يكون الله عز وجل ذكر أيدي، ورجع إلى إثبات يَدَيْن؛ لأن الدليل عنده دل على صحة الإجماع (۱) ، وإذا كان الإجماع صحيحاً وجب أن يرجع من قوله : أيد إلى يَدَيْن؛ لأن القرآن على ظاهره ، ولا يزول عن ظاهره إلا بحجة، فوجدنا حجة أزلنا بها ذكر الأيدي عن الظاهر إلى ظاهر آخر، ووجب أن يكون الظاهر الآخر على حقيقة لا يزول عنها إلا بحجة .

١٦٢ - مسألة :
فإن قال قائل : إذا ذكر الله عز وجل الأيدي وأراد يَدَيْن، فما أنكرتم أن يذكر الأيدي ويريد يداً واحدة؟
قيل له : ذكر الله عز وجل أيد وأراد يَدَيْن؛ لأنهم أجمعوا على بطلان قول من قال: أيد كثيرة، وقول من قال: يداً واحدة، فقلنا: يـدان؛ لأن القرآن على ظاهره، إلا أن تقوم حجة بأن يكون على خلاف الظاهر .

١٦٣ - مسألة :
فإن قال قائل: ما أنكرتم أن يكون قوله : مِمَّا عَمِلَتْ أَيْدِينَا ﴾ [يس: ٧١] وقوله تعالى : لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَيَّ ) على المجاز ؟
قيل له : حُكْمُ كلام الله عز وجل أن يكون على ظاهره وحقيقته، ولا يُخرج الشيء عن ظاهره إلى المجاز إلا بحجة ألا ترون أنه إذا كان ظاهر الكلام العموم، فإذا ورد بلفظ العموم والمراد به الخصوص، فليس هو على حقيقة الظاهر، وليس يجوز أن يعدل بما ظاهره العموم عن العموم بغیر حجة كذلك قول الله عز وجل : لِمَا خَلَقَتُ بِيَدَيَّ ) على ظاهره وحقيقته من إثبات اليدين ولا يجوز أن يعدل به عن ظاهر اليدين إلى مـا ادعاه خصومنا إلا بحجة، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز لمدع أن يدعي أن ما ظاهره العموم فهو على الخصوص، وما ظاهره الخصوص فهو على العموم بغـيـر حجة، وإذا لم يجز هذا لمدعيه بغير برهان لم يجز لكـم مـا ادعيتموه أنـه مجاز بغير حجة، بل واجب أن يكون قوله : لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَيَّ ) إثبات يَدَيْن الله تعالى في الحقيقة غير نعمتين، إذا كانت النعمتان لا يجوز عند أهل اللسان أن يقول قائلهم : فعلت بيدي، وهو يعني النعمتين
Issue: If they say: When you affirm for Allah two hands due to His saying 'for what I created with My two hands', why do you not affirm for Him hands (aydi) due to His saying 'from what Our hands (aydina) have made' [Ya Sin: 71]? It is said to them: They have unanimously agreed on the invalidity of the saying of whoever affirmed hands (aydi) for Allah. Since they unanimously agreed on the invalidity of that saying, it is necessary that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, mentioned 'hands' (aydi) and it reverts to the affirmation of two hands (yadayn); because the evidence in His view indicates the validity of consensus, and when consensus is valid it is necessary that His saying 'hands' (aydi) reverts to 'two hands' (yadayn); because the Qur'an is upon its apparent meaning, and it is not removed from its apparent meaning except by evidence. We found evidence by which we removed the mention of 'hands' (aydi) from the apparent meaning to another apparent meaning, and it is necessary that the other apparent meaning be upon reality, not removed from it except by evidence.

162 - Issue: If someone says: If Allah, Mighty and Majestic, mentioned 'hands' (aydi) and intended two hands (yadayn), what prevents you that He mentioned 'hands' (aydi) and intended a single hand? It is said to him: Allah, Mighty and Majestic, mentioned 'hands' (aydi) and intended two hands; because they unanimously agreed on the invalidity of the saying of whoever said many hands, and the saying of whoever said a single hand. So we say: two hands; because the Qur'an is upon its apparent meaning, unless evidence arises that it is contrary to the apparent meaning.

163 - Issue: If someone says: What prevents you that His saying 'from what Our hands (aydina) have made' [Ya Sin: 71] and His saying 'for what I created with My two hands' are metaphorical? It is said to him: The ruling of Allah's speech is that it is upon its apparent meaning and its reality, and a thing is not removed from its apparent meaning to metaphor except by evidence. Do you not see that if the apparent meaning of speech is generality, and it comes in the wording of generality while the intended meaning is specificity, then it is not upon the reality of the apparent meaning, and it is not permissible to turn what is apparently general away from generality without evidence. Similarly, Allah's saying 'for what I created with My two hands' is upon its apparent meaning and its reality of affirming two hands, and it is not permissible to turn it from the apparent meaning of two hands to what our opponents claim except by evidence. If that were permissible, it would be permissible for a claimant to claim that what is apparently general is specific, and what is apparently specific is general without evidence. And if this is not permissible for its claimant without proof, it is not permissible for you what you claim, that it is metaphor, without evidence. Rather, it is obligatory that His saying 'for what I created with My two hands' affirms two hands of Allah, Most High, in reality, not two blessings, since two blessings – it is not permissible, according to the people of language, for one of them to say 'I did it with my two hands' while meaning the two blessings.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents three objections and their answers defending the affirmation of two literal hands for Allah. The first objection argues: if the proof for two hands is the verse 'with My two hands', why not also affirm multiple hands based on the verse 'from what Our hands have made', where 'aydina' is plural? The answer relies on consensus: the community has agreed that affirming many hands is false. Therefore, the plural 'aydi' must refer to two hands, because the Qur'an is interpreted according to its apparent meaning (zahir) unless evidence requires departure. Consensus serves as that evidence, shifting the apparent meaning from 'multiple hands' to 'two hands', but the new apparent meaning is also real and cannot be changed without further evidence.

The second objection asks: if you interpret 'aydi' as two hands, why not interpret it as a single hand? The answer again invokes consensus: the community has agreed that both a single hand and many hands are invalid. Therefore, the only remaining possibility is two hands, maintaining the apparent meaning unless evidence to the contrary exists.

The third objection proposes that these verses might be metaphorical (majaz). The answer establishes a principle: Allah's speech is to be taken in its literal, real sense (haqiqah). Departure from the literal meaning to metaphor requires evidence. The argument uses an analogy: just as a general (aam) wording cannot be made specific (khaas) without evidence, so too the literal meaning of 'two hands' cannot be abandoned for a metaphorical interpretation without proof. If such metaphorical interpretations were allowed without evidence, then any text could be arbitrarily reinterpreted. Therefore, the verse must be taken literally as affirming two real hands, not two blessings (as some claimed). Al-Ashari emphasizes that language speakers do not say 'I did it with my two hands' while meaning two blessings; thus the literal meaning is the only valid one.

Key Terms
حقيقة
مجاز
ظاهر
عموم
خصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that the verses about Allah's hands are purely metaphorical and have no literal meaning.
Correction: Al-Ashari argues that metaphor requires evidence, and without it, the literal meaning stands.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Ashari, what is required to interpret a verse metaphorically rather than literally?
A) The consensus of scholars
B) Clear evidence (hujjah)
C) The opinion of the majority
D) The context of the verse

Key Takeaway
The default interpretation of Allah's speech is literal; metaphor is only acceptable when supported by clear evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse about Allah’s mercy or wrath, do you instinctively try to reinterpret it to fit your comfort zone, or do you let it confront you as it is? What evidence do you have for your reinterpretation — and would Al-Ashari accept it?
?
In your daily life, do you hold the same standard for Allah’s speech: that it means what it says unless there is clear proof otherwise? Or do you allow your own desires or cultural notions to turn verses from their apparent meaning without demanding evidence?
?
How often do you pause to examine whether you’ve accepted someone’s metaphorical explanation of a divine attribute without verifying the proof? Are you willing to challenge yourself intellectually and spiritually to take Allah’s words at face value, even when it makes you uncomfortable?

Refutation of the Metaphorical Interpretation of Yadayn
Al-Ash'ari refutes the metaphorical interpretation of 'with My two hands'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ash'ari insists on the literal interpretation of divine attributes like 'hands' and the requirement of evidence for metaphorical interpretation.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is literal by default; metaphor requires proof.

In this passage, al-Ash'ari defends the principle that Allah's words are to be understood in their apparent, literal sense unless clear evidence forces a metaphorical reading. He applies this to the verse 'with My two hands,' arguing that it affirms real hands for Allah, not mere blessings. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for theological methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Ash'ari structures his argument.
The passage uses a logical analogy.
2
Identify the principle
Find the rule that Allah's speech must be taken upon its apparent meaning and literal truth. Note that this is a foundational principle.
3
Examine the analogy
Al-Ash'ari draws an analogy with general and specific language. Understand how he uses this to show that a departure from the apparent meaning requires evidence.
The analogy is crucial for his argument.
4
Apply to the verse
See how the principle and analogy apply to the verse about 'two hands'. Al-Ash'ari concludes that the literal meaning of hands must be affirmed unless opponents bring evidence.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the final point: 'with My two hands' cannot mean two blessings because Arabic speakers do not use the phrase that way. This strengthens the literal interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله تعالى : لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَيَّ على المجاز ؟ قيل له : حُكْمُ كلام الله عز وجل أن يكون على ظاهره وحقيقته، ولا يُخرج الشيء عن ظاهره إلى المجاز إلا بحجة ألا ترون أنه إذا كان ظاهر الكلام العموم، فإذا ورد بلفظ العموم والمراد به الخصوص، فليس هو على حقيقة الظاهر، وليس يجوز أن يعدل بما ظاهره العموم عن العموم بغير حجة كذلك قول الله عز وجل : لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَيَّ على ظاهره وحقيقته من إثبات اليدين ولا يجوز أن يعدل به عن ظاهر اليدين إلى ما ادعاه خصومنا إلا بحجة، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز لمدع أن يدعي أن ما ظاهره العموم فهو على الخصوص، وما ظاهره الخصوص فهو على العموم بغير حجة، وإذا لم يجز هذا لمدعيه بغير برهان لم يجز لكم ما ادعيتموه أنه مجاز بغير حجة، بل واجب أن يكون قوله : لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَيَّ إثبات يَدَيْن لله تعالى في الحقيقة غير نعمتين، إذا كانت النعمتان لا يجوز عند أهل اللسان أن يقول قائلهم : فعلت بيدي، وهو يعني النعمتين
And His saying, the Most High: 'for what I created with My two hands' as metaphor? It is said to him: The rule of the speech of Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, is that it be upon its apparent meaning and its literal truth, and something is not removed from its apparent meaning to metaphor except with evidence. Do you not see that when the apparent meaning of speech is generality, and it comes with a general expression but the intended meaning is particular, then it is not upon the literal truth of the apparent meaning, and it is not permissible to turn what is apparently general away from generality without evidence. Similarly, the saying of Allah, the Mighty and Majestic: 'for what I created with My two hands' is upon its apparent meaning and its literal truth of affirming the two hands, and it is not permissible to turn it away from the apparent meaning of the two hands to what our opponents claim except with evidence. If that were permissible, then a claimant could claim that what is apparently general is particular and what is apparently particular is general without evidence. And since this is not permissible for its claimants without proof, it is not permissible for you to claim that it is metaphor without evidence. Rather, it is necessary that His saying: 'for what I created with My two hands' be an affirmation of two hands for Allah, the Most High, in reality, not two blessings, since when it comes to two blessings, it is not permissible for a speaker among the people of language to say: 'I did it with my two hands' while meaning the two blessings.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the metaphorical interpretation of the verse 'for what I created with My two hands'. The author argues that Allah's speech must be interpreted according to its apparent meaning and literal truth. He draws an analogy with the principle of generality and specificity in language: if a general expression is used but the intended meaning is specific, there must be evidence for that departure. Similarly, the apparent meaning of 'with My two hands' is the affirmation of two hands for Allah, and to interpret it metaphorically (e.g., as 'two blessings') requires clear evidence. If such free interpretation were allowed without evidence, then anyone could claim that general expressions are specific and vice versa, leading to chaos. He concludes that the phrase must be taken literally as affirming two real hands, not two blessings, because Arabic speakers do not use 'with my two hands' to mean 'with my two blessings'.

Correction: Al-Ash'ari argues that the apparent meaning is literal and requires evidence to interpret metaphorically; without evidence, the literal meaning stands.

Checkpoint

B) Evidence (hujjah)
C) Consensus of scholars
D) Contextual clues

Key Takeaway
The default interpretation of divine attributes is literal; metaphorical interpretation requires clear evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Quranic Evidence for Allah's Knowledge and Power
Al-Ash'ari cites Quranic verses to prove Allah's knowledge and power against the Jahmiyyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari uses explicit Quranic verses to affirm Allah's attributes of knowledge and power, directly refuting the Jahmiyyah's denial.

Core Idea
The Quran itself repeatedly affirms Allah's knowledge and power, refuting those who deny His attributes.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari directly counters the Jahmiyyah sect by citing multiple Quranic verses that explicitly mention Allah's knowledge and power. He demonstrates that the Quran uses terms like 'ilm (knowledge) and quwwa (strength) in various places, proving these attributes are established beyond doubt. This lesson will walk through these verses and their significance in the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: the chapter heading, then verses about knowledge, then verses about power.
The passage opens with a clear statement of refutation against the Jahmiyyah.
2
Identify the Attributes
Identify the two main attributes discussed: knowledge (علم) and power/strength (قوة). Count how many verses are cited for each.
Al-Ash'ari explicitly mentions that knowledge appears in five places.
3
Reflect on the Refutation
Consider how these verses directly counter the Jahmiyyah's claim. Why would citing multiple verses strengthen the argument?
The Jahmiyyah denied these attributes entirely; the Quran's repeated mention affirms them.
4
Analyze the Final Verse
Examine the verse about building the heaven 'with hands' (بأيد). Discuss how this also implies power or a literal hand, supporting the attribute of power.
This verse is often interpreted as a metaphor for power, but Al-Ash'ari uses it as evidence for an attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب التاسع الرد على الجهمية في نفيهم علم الله تعالى وقدرته وجميع صفاته قال الله عز وجل : ﴿ أَنزَلَهُ بِعِلْمِهِ ﴾ [النساء : ١٦٦] وقال تعالى: ﴿ وَمَا تَحْمِلُ مِنْ أُنثَى وَلَا تَضَعُ إِلَّا بِعِلْمِهِ ﴾ [فاطر: ١١]، وذكر العلم في خمسة مواضع من كتابه العزيز، وقال تعالى: ﴿ فَإِلَّمْ يَسْتَجِيبُوا لَكُمْ فَاعْلَمُوا أَنَّمَا أُنزِلَ بِعِلْمِ الله ﴾ [هود: ١٤]، وقال: ﴿ وَلَا يُحِيطُونَ بِشَيْءٍ مِنْ عِلْمِهِ إِلَّا بِمَا شَاءَ ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٥٥] وذكر القوة فقال : ﴿ أَوَلَمْ يَرَوْا أَنَّ اللَّهَ الَّذِى خَلَقَهُمْ هُوَ أَشَدُّ مِنْهُمْ قُوَّةً ﴾ [فصلت : ١٥] ، وقال تعالى: ﴿ ذُو الْقُوَّةِ الْمَتِينُ ﴾ [الذاريات : ٥٨] وقال تعالى: ﴿ وَالسَّماءَ بَنَيْناها بِأَيْدٍ ﴾ [الذاريات : ٤٧]
Chapter Nine: Refutation of the Jahmiyyah regarding their denial of the knowledge of Allah, the Most High, and His power and all His attributes. Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, said: 'He sent it down with His knowledge' [4:166]. And the Most High said: 'And no female conceives nor gives birth except with His knowledge' [35:11]. And He mentioned knowledge in five places of His Mighty Book. And the Most High said: 'If they do not respond to you, then know that it was sent down with the knowledge of Allah' [11:14]. And He said: 'They encompass nothing of His knowledge except what He wills' [2:255]. And He mentioned strength, saying: 'Do they not see that Allah who created them is stronger than them in power?' [41:15]. And the Most High said: 'Possessor of strength, the Mighty' [51:58]. And the Most High said: 'And the heaven We built with hands (ayd)' [51:47].
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage opens Chapter Nine, which aims to refute the Jahmiyyah who deny Allah's knowledge, power, and all attributes. Al-Ash'ari provides Quranic evidence: first, verses explicitly mentioning Allah's knowledge (4:166, 35:11, 11:14, 2:255), noting that knowledge is mentioned in five places. Then he cites verses about strength (quwwa) (41:15, 51:58) and finally a verse about building the heaven 'with hands' (ayd) (51:47), which implies power or actual hands. The citation establishes that the Quran itself affirms these attributes, directly countering the Jahmiyyah's denial.

Quranic Evidence for Allah's Knowledge and Power
Imam Ash'ari quotes Quranic verses as evidence for Allah's knowledge and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ash'ari uses explicit Quranic verses to affirm Allah's attributes of knowledge and power against those who deny them.

Core Idea
The Quran itself explicitly affirms Allah's knowledge and power, refuting those who deny them.

Imam Ash'ari begins this chapter by directly confronting the Jahmiyyah, a group that denied Allah's attributes. He does not argue with philosophy; he simply presents the Quranic verses that explicitly mention Allah's knowledge and power. These verses serve as clear, undeniable proof that these attributes belong to Allah. As you read, notice how the author lets the Quran speak for itself.

Guided Path

الباب التاسع: الرد على الجهمية في نفيهم علم الله تعالى وقدرته وجميع صفاته. قال الله عز وجل: {أَنزَلَهُ بِعِلْمِهِ} [النساء: 166] وقال تعالى: {وَمَا تَحْمِلُ مِنْ أُنثَى وَلَا تَضَعُ إِلَّا بِعِلْمِهِ} [فاطر: 11]، وذكر العلم في خمسة مواضع من كتابه العزيز، وقال تعالى: {فَإِن لَّمْ يَسْتَجِيبُوا لَكُمْ فَاعْلَمُوا أَنَّمَا أُنزِلَ بِعِلْمِ اللهِ} [هود: 14]، وقال: {وَلَا يُحِيطُونَ بِشَيْءٍ مِنْ عِلْمِهِ إِلَّا بِمَا شَاءَ} [البقرة: 255] وذكر القوة فقال: {أَوَلَمْ يَرَوْا أَنَّ اللَّهَ الَّذِي خَلَقَهُمْ هُوَ أَشَدُّ مِنْهُمْ قُوَّةً} [فصلت: 15]، وقال تعالى: {ذُو الْقُوَّةِ الْمَتِينُ} [الذاريات: 58] وقال تعالى: {وَالسَّمَاءَ بَنَيْنَاهَا بِأَيْدٍ وَإِنَّا لَمُوسِعُونَ} [الذاريات: 47].
Chapter Nine: Refutation of the Jahmiyyah regarding their denial of Allah's knowledge, power, and all His attributes. Allah Almighty said: 'He revealed it with His knowledge' (4:166), and He said: 'No female conceives nor gives birth except with His knowledge' (35:11), and He mentioned knowledge in five places of His Noble Book, and He said: 'If they do not respond to you, then know that it was revealed with the knowledge of Allah' (11:14), and He said: 'They encompass nothing of His knowledge except what He wills' (2:255), and He mentioned power, saying: 'Do they not see that Allah who created them is stronger than them in power?' (41:15), and He said: 'Possessor of power, the Strong' (51:58), and He said: 'And the heaven We built it with hands, and indeed We are expanding' (51:47).

Commentary
This passage is a collection of Quranic proofs for Allah's knowledge and power. Imam Ash'ari cites multiple verses to demonstrate that Allah explicitly affirms His own knowledge and power. He mentions that knowledge is referenced in five places (though only four are quoted here, implying familiarity with the Quran). The verses from Surat an-Nisa', Fatir, Hud, al-Baqarah, Fussilat, and adh-Dhariyat serve as evidence against the Jahmiyyah who denied these attributes. The inclusion of both knowledge and power shows that the Quran itself refutes their claims.

D) Ten

Key Takeaway
The Quran provides clear, explicit proof that Allah possesses knowledge and power as essential attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that every pregnancy and birth occurs only with Allah's knowledge, how does that confrontation change the way you view your own origin and existence? Will you now submit to His knowledge in every detail of your life?
?
When you feel powerless in a situation, do you recall that Allah is 'Possessor of power, the Strong' and that He built the heavens with His might? How can you turn your weakness into trust in His power today?
?
The verse 'They encompass nothing of His knowledge except what He wills' means you can never fully grasp God. Does this limitation humble you or frustrate you? Will you accept your place as a dependent creature or rebel against your limits?

Mu'tazila's Nominal Affirmation of Attributes
Al-Ash'ari critiques Mu'tazila's nominal affirmation of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's position on divine attributes: they affirmed the names but denied the realities behind them.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila said 'God is Knowing' but denied real knowledge in God.

Al-Ash'ari exposes an inconsistency in the Mu'tazila's theology. They were unwilling to deny God's attributes outright, so they used the names while rejecting their underlying realities. This passage shows their attempt to maintain orthodoxy while deviating from it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Let the author's words speak for themselves.
Notice the phrase 'by way of naming'.
2
Identify the contradiction
The Mu'tazila affirm that God is 'Knowing, Powerful, Living, Hearing, Seeing' but deny the reality of knowledge, power, hearing, and sight. Identify this tension.
3
Understand the implication
Reflect on what it means to use a name without its corresponding reality. Is the name meaningful without the reality?
This is the crux of Al-Ash'ari's critique.
4
Connect to broader theology
Consider how this relates to the Islamic debate on divine attributes. Why does it matter whether attributes are real or merely nominal?
This connects to the wider Ahl al-Sunnah position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بصير، فلم تقدر المعتزلة أن تفصح بذلك فأتت بمعناه، وقالت: سميع إن الله عالم قادر حي سميع بصير من طريق التسمية من غير أن يثبتوا له حقيقة العلم والقدرة والسمع والبصر
And not seeing, so the Mu'tazila could not openly say that, so they came with its meaning, and said: Hearing, that God is Knowing, Powerful, Living, Hearing, Seeing by way of naming, without affirming for Him the reality of knowledge, power, hearing, and sight.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage explains the Mu'tazila's position on divine attributes. They affirmed the names (e.g., Samī, Baṣīr) but denied that these names correspond to real attributes inherent in God. Al-Ash'ari points out their inconsistency: they use the terms but empty them of their true meanings.

Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila completely denied all divine attributes, leaving God with no names or descriptions.
Correction: The text shows they affirmed the names (e.g., Samī', Baṣīr) but denied the realities behind them.

The Mu'tazila affirmed divine names but denied they correspond to real attributes in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The Mu'tazila said 'God is Knowing' but denied the reality of knowledge in Him. What would it mean for you to live out the reality of His power in a specific challenge you are facing right now?
?
Al-Ash'ari exposes the emptiness of nominal faith: naming without reality. In your prayer and worship, are there 'names' you recite—like 'Allah is Merciful'—without letting that mercy transform your heart and dealings with others? How can you turn your recitation of His attributes into a life that truly reflects their reality?

The Absurd Consequence of Identifying Knowledge with God
Al-Ash'ari relates and refutes Abu al-Hudhayl on knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contradiction in Abu al-Hudhayl's claim that God's knowledge is identical to God, as exposed by Imam al-Ash'ari.

Core Idea
If knowledge is God, then praying to knowledge is praying to God.

Imam al-Ash'ari recounts a specific Mu'tazilite view that collapses the distinction between God and His attribute of knowledge. By showing the absurd practical implication, he demonstrates the internal inconsistency of this position. This lesson focuses on that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Abu al-Hudhayl
Read the Arabic text about Abu al-Hudhayl's claim and the response. Notice the structure: claim, implication, refusal, conclusion.
The key phrase is 'فجعل الله تعالى علما'.
2
Identify the logical reduction
Al-Ash'ari reduces the claim to an absurdity: if knowledge = God, then calling upon 'Knowledge of God' is calling upon God. Abu al-Hudhayl's refusal to accept this shows his inconsistency.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider why this refutation is effective: it forces the opponent to either accept an absurd consequence or abandon his original claim.
Al-Ash'ari uses the opponent's own rejection to prove contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قال رئيس من رؤسائهم ـ وهو أبو الهذيل العلاف ـ إن علم الله هو الله، فجعل الله تعالى علماً . وأُلْزِمَ، فقيل له : إذا قلت إن علم الله هو الله فقل: يا علــم الله اغفر لي وارحمني، فأبى ذلك، فلزمه المناقضة . واعلموا - رحمكم الله - أن من قال عالم ولا علم كان مناقضاً، كما أن من قال علم ولا عالم كان مناقضاً ، وكذلك القول في القدرة والقادر، والحياة والحي، والسمع والبصر والسميع والبصير.
And one of their leaders — Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf — said that God's knowledge is God, so he made God, exalted, knowledge. And he was compelled, and it was said to him: If you say that God's knowledge is God, then say: O knowledge of God, forgive me and have mercy on me. But he refused that, so contradiction befell him. And know — may God have mercy on you — that whoever says 'knowing' without knowledge is contradictory, just as whoever says 'knowledge' without a knower is contradictory, and likewise the statement regarding power and the powerful, life and the living, hearing and sight and the hearing and seeing.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents a specific Mu'tazili view and its refutation. Abu al-Hudhayl claimed that God's knowledge is identical to His essence, so that 'knowledge' is just another name for God. Al-Ash'ari shows the absurd consequence: if knowledge = God, then calling upon 'Knowledge of God' would be calling upon God. But Abu al-Hudhayl refused to accept that implication, which shows inconsistency. Then al-Ash'ari generalizes: just as 'knowing' without knowledge is contradictory, so 'knowledge' without a knower is contradictory. The same applies to other attributes: the terms imply each other.

Key Terms
أبو الهذيل العلاف
علم الله
مناقضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's knowledge is identical to His essence in a way that eliminates any distinction.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows this leads to absurdity; attributes and essence are distinct yet inseparable.

Checkpoint
What consequence did al-Ash'ari draw from Abu al-Hudhayl's claim?
A) That knowledge is an accident.
B) That one could pray to 'Knowledge of God'.
C) That God has no knowledge.
D) That attributes are separate from essence.

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Hudhayl's identification of knowledge with God leads to an absurdity that even he rejects, thus his position is self-refuting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking of Allah's attributes as if they were separate beings or powers alongside Allah? How does this refutation of Abu al-Hudhayl correct that tendency in your own heart?
?
If someone were to ask you to pray to 'the knowledge of Allah' or 'the mercy of Allah,' how would you respond? Does your answer reflect a firm understanding that only Allah is to be worshipped?
?
Abu al-Hudhayl refused to pray to knowledge because he knew in his heart it was wrong. Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah that, if you were honest, lead to practical consequences you would never accept? Will you examine those today?

Attributes and Their Subjects Are Inseparable
Al-Ash'ari states the general rule of inseparability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the general principle that a subject without its attribute is as contradictory as an attribute without its subject, as taught by Imam al-Ash'ari.

Core Idea
Knowing without knowledge is as contradictory as knowledge without a knower.

After refuting Abu al-Hudhayl, Imam al-Ash'ari lays down a universal principle: the terms for attributes and their subjects imply each other. Just as 'knowledge' requires a knower, so 'knowing' requires knowledge. This principle applies to all divine attributes and safeguards orthodox theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the general principle
Read the second part of the passage starting from 'واعلموا'. Notice the parallelism between knowing/knowledge, power/powerful, etc.
Al-Ash'ari uses a logical symmetry.
2
Identify the two directions
The principle states two things: 1) claiming 'knowing' without knowledge is contradictory, and 2) claiming 'knowledge' without a knower is contradictory. Both are equally invalid.
This refutes both those who deny attributes and those who attribute without subject.
3
Apply to other attributes
Al-Ash'ari explicitly extends the same logic to power, life, hearing, and sight. See if you can articulate the pattern.
The same reasoning applies to all essential attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And one of their leaders — Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf — said that God's knowledge is God, so he made God, exalted, knowledge. And he was compelled, and it was said to him: If you say that God's knowledge is God, then say: O knowledge of God, forgive me and have mercy on me. But he refused that, so contradiction befell him. And know — may God have mercy on you — that whoever says 'knowing' without knowledge is contradictory, just as whoever says 'knowledge' without a knower is contradictory, and likewise the statement regarding power and the powerful, life and the living, hearing and sight and the hearing and seeing.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents a specific Mu'tazili view and its refutation. Abu al-Hudhayl claimed that God's knowledge is identical to His essence, so that 'knowledge' is just another name for God. Al-Ash'ari shows the absurd consequence: if knowledge = God, then calling upon 'Knowledge of God' would be calling upon God. But Abu al-Hudhayl refused to accept that implication, which shows inconsistency. Then al-Ash'ari generalizes: just as 'knowing' without knowledge is contradictory, so 'knowledge' without a knower is contradictory. The same applies to other attributes: the terms imply each other.

The Proof that Allah's Knowledge is a Real Attribute
Al-Ash'ari proves that describing God as 'knowing' requires affirming a real attribute of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari uses logical analogy and early Muslim consensus to prove that describing Allah as 'knowing' necessarily entails affirming a real attribute of knowledge.

Core Idea
If describing Allah as 'speaking' requires speech, then describing Him as 'knowing' requires knowledge.

Al-Ash'ari presents a powerful argument by analogy. He first asks the opponents to consider a hypothetical: someone who claims Allah is speaking yet denies He has speech would be deemed contradictory and outside the Muslim community. The opponents must agree. Then he applies the same logic to the attribute of knowledge: claiming Allah is knowing without affirming a real attribute of knowledge is equally contradictory. He then bolsters this with the consensus of early Muslims before the rise of the sects, who consistently affirmed that Allah has eternal and prior knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the gist of the argument.
Notice the rhetorical structure: the author addresses opponents directly.
2
Identify the reductio argument
Find the hypothetical about someone claiming Allah is speaking but without speech. Ask yourself why the opponents must say yes.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the analogical application
Notice how Al-Ash'ari applies the same logic to knowledge: if knowing without knowledge is contradictory, then the attribute must be real.
The analogy is the core of the proof.
4
Examine the consensus argument
Read the part about the early Muslims agreeing on Allah's eternal and prior knowledge. This shows that denying knowledge is an innovation.
Consensus (ijma') is a key source of authority in Sunni Islam.
5
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does this matter? The attribute deniers are shown to be contradictory and outside the mainstream.
This defense of attributes protects the concept of Allah's perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم : خبرونا عمن زعم أن الله متكلم قائل آمر ناه، لا قول له ولا كلام ولا أمر له ولا نهي أليس هو مناقض خارج عن جملة المسلمين؟ فلا بد من نعم، فيقال لهم : فكذلك من قال : إن الله تعالى عالم ولا علم له كان ذلك مناقضاً خارجاً عن جملة المسلمين . وقد أجمع المسلمون قبل حدوث الجهمية والمعتزلة والحرورية على أن الله علماً لم يزل، وقد قالوا علم الله لم يزل، وعلم الله سابق في الأشياء، ولا يمنعون أن يقولوا في كل حادثة تحدث، ونازلة تنزل: كل هذا سابق في علم الله ، فمن جحد أن الله علماً فقد خالف المسلمين وخرج عن اتفاقهم
And it is said to them: Inform us about one who claims that God is speaking, saying, commanding, forbidding, while He has no speech, no utterance, no command, and no prohibition — is he not contradictory, outside the community of Muslims? So they must say yes. Then it is said to them: Likewise, whoever says that God, exalted, is knowing but has no knowledge, that is contradictory, outside the community of Muslims. And the Muslims agreed before the emergence of the Jahmiyya, Mu'tazila, and Haruriyya that God has eternal knowledge, and they said: God's knowledge is eternal, and God's knowledge is prior in things, and they do not prevent saying regarding every occurring event and descending matter: all of this is prior in God's knowledge. So whoever denies that God has knowledge has opposed the Muslims and departed from their consensus.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy and consensus. Al-Ash'ari first uses a reductio: if someone claimed God is speaking but without speech, he would be considered contradictory and outside Islam. The opponent must agree. Then he applies the same logic to 'knowing' without knowledge. Then he invokes the consensus of early Muslims (before the heretical sects) that God has eternal knowledge, and they consistently spoke of God's prior knowledge of events. Thus denying the attribute of knowledge is a departure from the Muslim consensus.

Checkpoint

B) That God's knowledge is eternal and prior.
C) That God has no knowledge.
D) That knowledge is an accident.

Key Takeaway
Denying the attribute of knowledge while affirming that Allah is knowing is as contradictory as claiming He speaks without speech, and contradicts the consensus of the early Muslims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How would your behavior change if you internalized that Allah's knowledge is a real attribute that sees even your hidden intentions? Commit to a specific change.

This passage shows the absurdity of claiming Allah is knowing but without knowledge. Is there any attribute of Allah you affirm with your tongue but deny in your heart by not living accordingly? What would it mean for you to truly believe that Allah's knowledge is real and eternal? How would that reshape your daily decisions?
?
Al-Ash'ari warns that denying the attribute of knowledge puts one outside the community of Muslims and its consensus. Have you ever considered that your own casual dismissal of divine attributes might be a slippery slope? What step can you take today to deepen your conviction in all of Allah's attributes, not just the ones that feel comfortable?
