Analogy between will and knowledge
Al-Ash'ari parallels the necessity of will and knowledge as divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari uses an analogy between will and knowledge to argue that knowledge is a real attribute of God.

Core Idea
If willing requires will, then knowing requires knowledge.

Al-Ash'ari presents a concise logical argument by analogy. He challenges the Mu'tazila who affirm that God is willing but might deny that He has knowledge as an attribute. The argument forces them to choose between consistency and absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage once without analysis. Notice the rhetorical question and the two possible responses.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the rhetorical structure
Identify the question, the hypothetical answer (if they say no), and the conclusion drawn from each branch.
3
Trace the analogy
See how the attribute of will (irāda) is paralleled with knowledge (ʿilm). The same logic that makes will necessary also makes knowledge necessary.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument establishes the reality of divine attributes against those who deny them.
This argument is a classic example of qiyās al-ghāʾib ʿalā al-shāhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم : إذا كان الله مريداً أفله إرادة ؟ فإن قالوا: لا، قيل لهم : فإذا أثبتم مريداً لا إرادة له، فأثبتوا قائلاً لا قول له، وإن أثبتوا الإرادة قيل لهم : فإذا كان المريد لا يكون مريداً إلا بإرادة، فما أنكرتم أن لا يكون العالم عالماً إلا بعلم، وأن يكون الله علم كما أثبتم له إرادة .
And it is said to them: If God is willing, does He have will? If they say: No, it is said to them: So you have affirmed a willing one without will, so affirm a speaker without speech. And if they affirm will, it is said to them: Since the willing is not willing except with will, what prevents you that the knower is not knowing except with knowledge, and that God has knowledge as you have affirmed for Him will.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage uses an argument from analogy. The Mu'tazila likely affirm that God is willing (murīd) and that He has a will (irāda). Al-Ash'ari asks them: if you affirm a will for the attribute of willing, then why not affirm knowledge for the attribute of knowing? The logic is parallel: just as 'willing' requires a real attribute 'will', so 'knowing' requires a real attribute 'knowledge'. The opponent is forced to either deny will (which is absurd) or accept the parallel, thus affirming knowledge.

Key Terms
مريد
إرادة
عالم
علم
قائل
قول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God has will as an attribute but does not have knowledge as a distinct attribute.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows by analogy that if will is a real attribute, then knowledge must also be real, because both 'willing' and 'knowing' are descriptions that require an underlying attribute. Consistency demands acceptance of both.

Checkpoint
What does al-Ash'ari use as an analogy to prove that knowledge is an attribute?
A) Power
B) Life
C) Will
D) Speech

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash’ari uses the analogy of will to establish that knowledge is a real, distinct attribute of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if God’s knowledge of you is as real and complete as His will? When you sin in secret, do you remember that the One who wills your repentance also knows your every thought? What would change in your daily life if you internalized that God’s knowledge is not a concept but an ever-present reality?
?
If you accept that God is all-knowing, why do you often act as if He is unaware of your struggles or your needs? Are you willing to let go of the illusion of privacy and stand exposed before His knowledge? What would it look like for you to place your full trust in a God whose knowledge includes every detail of your future?
?
The argument forces you to admit: if you accept God’s will, you must also accept His knowledge. But does your heart treat both attributes equally? When you pray, do you ask God to will something for you without truly believing He already knows what is best? What would change in your dua if you prayed with the certainty that His knowledge is perfect?

Divine Attributes

The Analogy Between Divine Knowledge and Speech
Al-Ash'ari refutes the Mu'tazili distinction between knowledge and speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari refutes the Mu'tazili claim that speech is an attribute but knowledge is not, using the example of God teaching Moses and Pharaoh.

Core Idea
If particular speech proves speech as an attribute, particular teaching must prove knowledge as an attribute.

The Mu'tazila argued that God's speech to Moses alone proved speech is a distinct attribute, but they denied the same for knowledge. Al-Ash'ari turns their own logic against them: if teaching Moses alone implies knowledge as an attribute, then God's general teaching implies a general attribute of knowledge. In this passage, he presents two arguments to refute their distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili claim
Read the passage carefully. The Mu'tazila claim that God taught Moses and Pharaoh, but spoke only to Moses. They use this to argue that speech is an attribute but knowledge is not.
Note that they accept teaching as an act but deny it implies an attribute.
2
Understand the first analogy
Al-Ash'ari says: if speaking to Moses alone shows God has speech, then teaching Moses alone should show God has knowledge. The same logic applies.
3
Follow the second argument
He then argues: if God taught both Moses and Pharaoh, then He must have knowledge that applies to both equally. Similarly, God spoke to all things by saying 'Be', so He has a general attribute of speech.
This shows consistency in the doctrine of attributes.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The Mu'tazili distinction is refuted: both knowledge and speech are attributes of God, proven by the same reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد فرقوا بين العلم والكلام فقالوا: إن الله عز وجل علم موسى وفرعون، وكلم موسى ولم يكلم فرعون، فكذلك قد يقال: علم موسى الحكمة وفصل الخطاب، وآتاه النبوة، ولم يعلم ذلك فرعون فإن كان لله كلام؛ لأنه كلم موسى ولم يكلم فرعون، فكذلك لله علم؛ لأنه علم موسى ولم يعلم فرعون . ثم يقال لهم: إذا وجب أن لله كلاماً به كلم موسى دون فرعون إذ كلم موسى دونه، فما أنكرتم إذا علمهما جميعاً أن يكون له علم به علمهما جميعاً . ثم يقال: قد كلم الله الأشياء بأن قال لها: كوني، وقد أثبتم الله قولاً، فكذلك إن علم الأشياء كلها، فله علم
And they made a distinction between knowledge and speech and said: God, mighty and majestic, taught Moses and Pharaoh, and spoke to Moses but did not speak to Pharaoh. So likewise it may be said: He taught Moses wisdom and decisive speech, and gave him prophethood, and He did not teach that to Pharaoh. If God has speech because He spoke to Moses and did not speak to Pharaoh, then similarly God has knowledge because He taught Moses and did not teach Pharaoh. Then it is said to them: Since it is necessary that God has speech by which He spoke to Moses without Pharaoh, as He spoke to Moses to the exclusion of him, what prevents you, when He taught both of them, that He has knowledge by which He knew both of them together? Then it is said: God has spoken to things by saying to them 'Be', and you have affirmed that God has a 'saying' (qawl). So likewise, since He knows all things, He has knowledge.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage addresses a Mu'tazili argument that distinguishes between speech and knowledge. The Mu'tazila claimed that God spoke to Moses but not to Pharaoh, proving that speech is a real attribute (since it is particular), but they did not infer a similar attribute for knowledge from the fact that God taught Moses but not Pharaoh. Al-Ash'ari argues: if the act of speaking to one person rather than another shows that speech is an attribute, then the act of teaching one person rather than another should show that knowledge is an attribute. He strengthens the argument: if God taught both, then the knowledge is the same, so it must be an attribute. And finally, God spoke to all things by commanding 'Be', which implies a general attribute of speech; similarly, God's knowledge of all things implies a general attribute of knowledge.

Key Terms
علم
كلام
تكليم
تعليم
موسى
فرعون
قول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The act of particular speaking proves speech as an attribute but particular teaching does not prove knowledge as an attribute.
Correction: If particular speech proves speech, particular teaching must prove knowledge, and general teaching implies general knowledge.

Checkpoint
How does al-Ash'ari refute the distinction between knowledge and speech?
A) By denying that God spoke to Moses.
B) By arguing that teaching is analogous to speaking.
C) By claiming knowledge is not an attribute.
D) By quoting consensus.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari demonstrates that the Mu'tazili logic about speech being an attribute due to particularity applies equally to knowledge, thus refuting their distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila applied a selective standard: they accepted that particular speech proved speech as an attribute but denied that particular teaching proved knowledge. Where in your life have you applied a double standard to avoid acknowledging God's complete sovereignty or attributes? Will you stop now and ask God to grant you consistency in your faith?
?
Al-Ash'ari used the fact that God taught both Moses and Pharaoh to argue for a general attribute of knowledge. When you see God granting knowledge or wisdom to someone, do you treat it as a mere event or as a reflection of His eternal knowledge? How can you change your perspective today to see His attributes behind every act?
?
If God's speech to all things by 'Be' is an attribute, and His knowledge of all things is also an attribute, then nothing in your life escapes His knowledge or is beyond His speaking. Are there areas of your life you try to hide from His knowledge or from His command? Will you repent and surrender those areas to Him now?

The Opponents' Admission Proves God's Knowledge
Al-Ash'ari argues that opponents admit God's knowledge of events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari uses the opponents' own admission to argue for God's attribute of knowledge.

Core Idea
Admission of divine foreknowledge implies the existence of the attribute of knowledge.

Al-Ash'ari presents a concise logical argument: his opponents, who deny that God has an attribute of knowledge, nonetheless admit that every event is known to God beforehand. This admission, he argues, logically entails that God indeed has knowledge. The argument serves to ground divine attributes in consensus and rationality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how the argument is condensed into a single sentence.
2
Identify the premise
Find the opponents' admission: that every event is 'prior in the knowledge of God.' This is the starting point of the argument.
The phrase 'سابق في علم الله' (prior in the knowledge of God) is key.
3
Understand the conclusion
From the admission, Al-Ash'ari draws two conclusions: (1) God has knowledge, and (2) denying this opposes Muslim consensus.
The conclusion is implicit but clear: if events are known to God, God must have knowledge.
4
Reflect on the role of consensus
Why does Al-Ash'ari mention consensus? He uses it to show that the opponents' own admission aligns with mainstream Islam, exposing their inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يمنعون أن يقولوا في كل حادثة تحدث، ونازلة تنزل: كل هذا سابق في علم الله ، فمن جحد أن الله علماً فقد خالف المسلمين وخرج عن اتفاقهم
And they do not prevent saying concerning every event that occurs and every calamity that befalls: all this is prior in the knowledge of God. So whoever denies that God has knowledge has opposed the Muslims and left their consensus.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument: The opponents (likely Mu'tazila or others) admit that every event is known to God beforehand. Therefore, they implicitly acknowledge that God has knowledge. Hence, anyone who denies that God has the attribute of knowledge is contradicting the consensus of Muslims. The author is using the opponents' own admission to prove that God has an attribute of knowledge.

Key Terms
علم (knowledge)
حادثة (event)
سابق (prior)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument relies solely on consensus without providing logical proof.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari first establishes a logical premise (the opponents' own admission) before invoking consensus, making the argument logically sound and not merely an appeal to authority.

Checkpoint
What do the opponents admit according to this passage?
A) That God is all-powerful
B) That every event is prior in God's knowledge
C) That God does not have knowledge
D) That consensus is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
The opponents' own admission that every event is known to God proves that God possesses the attribute of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every single event in your life—every joy, every hardship, every mistake—was already known to Allah before it occurred? How does this awareness change the way you react when something unexpected happens? Commit to repeating 'Allahu ʿalim bi kulli shay' (Allah knows all things) when you feel surprised.
?
When you face a calamity (nāzila), do you instinctively feel that Allah is unaware of your suffering, or do you immediately acknowledge His foreknowledge? If you struggle with the latter, what practical step can you take today to strengthen your conviction in His encompassing knowledge?
?
If you were to deny that Allah has the attribute of knowledge, you would be leaving the consensus of the Muslims and opposing the very premise that you accept in your daily speech. Is there any doubt in your heart about Allah’s knowledge that you have been ignoring? Resolve to seek refuge from doubt and affirm with certainty: 'Allah knows all things before they are.'

The Argument from Will to Knowledge
Al-Ash'ari refutes the denial of God's knowledge via analogy with will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari uses the analogy of will to prove that God has knowledge, and the logical structure of his refutation.

Core Idea
If God has will, then He must have knowledge — the same logic applies.

Al-Ash'ari presents a concise logical argument against those who affirm that God is a willer but deny that He is knowing. By drawing an analogy between will and knowledge, he shows that consistency demands affirming both or falling into absurdity. This passage exemplifies the kalam method of dialectical refutation using reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: it presents a dilemma with two horns.
2
Identify the two branches of the dilemma
The first branch is if the opponents deny that God has will; the second is if they affirm He has will. Identify the consequence of each branch.
Look for the words 'فإن قالوا: لا' and 'وإن أثبتوا الإرادة'.
3
Understand the logical inference from will to knowledge
See how the second branch leads to the conclusion that God must have knowledge, using the analogy that just as a willer requires will, a knower requires knowledge.
This is an argument by analogy (qiyas), a common method in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم : إذا كان الله مريداً أفله إرادة ؟ فإن قالوا: لا، قيل لهم : فإذا أثبتم مريداً لا إرادة له، فأثبتوا قائلاً لا قول له، وإن أثبتوا الإرادة قيل لهم : فإذا كان المريد لا يكون مريداً إلا بإرادة، فما أنكرتم أن لا يكون العالم عالماً إلا بعلم، وأن يكون الله علم كما أثبتم له إرادة
And it is said to them: If God is a willer, does He have will? If they say: No, it is said to them: So if you affirm a willer without will, then affirm a speaker without speech. And if they affirm will, it is said to them: Since the willer is only a willer by will, why do you deny that the knower is only knowing by knowledge, and that God has knowledge just as you affirm He has will?
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a logical rebuttal (جواب) against opponents who deny that God has knowledge while affirming that He is a willer (مريد). The argument proceeds by dilemma: If they say God does not have will while being a willer, then by analogy they must also accept a speaker without speech, which is absurd. If they affirm that God has will, then they must also affirm that God has knowledge, because just as a willer requires will, a knower requires knowledge. The conclusion is that God has knowledge as an attribute.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
مريد (willer)
عالم (knowing)
علم (knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only works if opponents accept the analogy of will to knowledge.
Correction: It is a reductio ad absurdum based on their own position: they already accept that God is a willer. Consistency forces them to accept knowledge or face absurdity.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what do the opponents implicitly affirm if they say God has will?
A) That God has power
B) That God has knowledge
C) That God has speech
D) That God has no attributes

Key Takeaway
By analogical reasoning, affirming that God has will necessitates affirming that He has knowledge, because both are attributes that define the agent's actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe God knows every detail of your life, including your private thoughts and future choices? Or do you sometimes live as if He doesn't—ignoring His knowledge when you sin or plan without seeking His guidance? This lesson forces you to be consistent: if you affirm God's will, you must affirm His knowledge. How will you change your daily awareness to reflect that He truly knows?
?
The argument shows that denying one attribute leads to absurdity. Have you ever selectively believed in only the comfortable attributes of God—like His mercy—while ignoring His knowledge or justice? Examine your heart: are there areas where you resist God's full description? What would it mean for you to accept all His attributes equally?
?
If you truly internalized that God's knowledge is as real as His will, how would it change the way you repent? Would you be more honest about your faults, knowing He sees them anyway? This lesson calls you to live with the reality of God's knowledge—not as a concept, but as a constant witness. Write down one specific area where you will start living differently because of this truth.

The Opponents' Distinction Between Knowledge and Speech
The opponents' argument distinguishing knowledge from speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument used by the opponents to distinguish between divine knowledge and speech and how they conclude that speech is an attribute while knowledge is not.

Core Idea
Opponents argue that God's selective speech proves an attribute, but His teaching both does not prove knowledge as an attribute.

In this passage, al-Ash'ari presents the position of a group that distinguishes between the attributes of knowledge and speech. They use the example of God teaching Moses and Pharaoh versus speaking only to Moses to argue that speech is a real attribute while knowledge is not. Pay close attention to how they reason from divine actions to divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the opponents start with factual premises about God's actions.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find the distinction the opponents make between teaching and speaking. They note that God taught both Moses and Pharaoh but spoke only to Moses.
This distinction is the basis of their whole argument.
3
Notice how they conclude
The opponents conclude that because speech is specific to Moses, it implies a speech attribute. Similarly, because teaching is specific to Moses (actually they taught both, but they focus on the selective aspect?), they claim knowledge is not an attribute? The text is subtle.
The opponents aim to deny knowledge as an attribute, even though their reasoning might seem to imply it.
4
Ask yourself: is this reasoning consistent?
Reflect on whether the opponents' reasoning is logically sound. The next lesson will show al-Ash'ari's response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد فرقوا بين العلم والكلام فقالوا: إن الله عز وجل علم موسى وفرعون، وكلم موسى ولم يُكلِّم فرعون، فكذلك قد يقال: عَلَّم موسى الحكمة وفصل الخطاب، وآتاه النبوة، ولم يُعلم ذلك فرعون فإن كان الله كلام؛ لأنه كلَّم موسى ولم يكلم فرعون، فكذلك الله علم ؛ لأنه علم موسى ولم يعلم فرعون . ثم يقال لهم: إذا وجب أن الله كلاماً به كلم موسى دون فرعون إذ كلَّم موسى دونه، فما أنكرتم إذا علمهما جميعاً أن يكون له علم به علمهما جميعاً . ثم يقال: قد كَلَّم الله الأشياء بأن قال لها: كوني، وقد أثبتم الله قولاً، فكذلك إن علم الأشياء كلها، فله علم
And they distinguished between knowledge and speech, saying: God taught Moses and Pharaoh, and spoke to Moses but did not speak to Pharaoh. So likewise it may be said: He taught Moses wisdom and decisive speech, and gave him prophecy, but did not teach that to Pharaoh. So if God had speech because He spoke to Moses and not to Pharaoh, then likewise God had knowledge because He taught Moses and did not teach Pharaoh. Then it is said to them: If it is necessary that God had speech by which He spoke to Moses without Pharaoh, since He spoke to Moses and not him, then why do you deny that when He taught both, He had knowledge by which He taught both? Then it is said: God spoke to things by saying 'Be', and you affirm that God has speech, so likewise if He knows all things, He has knowledge.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a 'problem' (مسألة) posed by the opponents, and al-Ash'ari's response. The opponents distinguish between knowledge and speech: God taught both Moses and Pharaoh, but spoke only to Moses; they argue from this that God has speech (because He spoke to one) but not necessarily knowledge (since He taught both, they might admit knowledge, but they want to deny it?). Actually the text is tricky: They say 'if God had speech because He spoke to Moses and not Pharaoh, then likewise God had knowledge because He taught Moses and not Pharaoh' — this is the opponents' own reasoning? Or is al-Ash'ari stating his opponents' position? Looking carefully: The opponents 'distinguished' (فَرَّقُوا) and said: God taught Moses and Pharaoh, and spoke to Moses not Pharaoh, so accordingly it may be said (فكذلك قد يقال): He taught Moses wisdom... So they are arguing that just as speech is specific to Moses, so knowledge is specific? Then al-Ash'ari responds: If it is necessary that God has speech by which He spoke to Moses, then why do you deny that when He taught both (Moses and Pharaoh), He has knowledge by which He taught both? Then another reponse: God spoke to things by 'Be', and you affirm speech, so likewise if He knows all things, He has knowledge. So al-Ash'ari is refuting their distinction and showing consistency requires affirming knowledge.

Key Terms
علم (knowledge)
كلام (speech)
تعليم (teaching)
تكليم (speaking to)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the opponents are affirming knowledge as an attribute because they say 'then likewise God had knowledge'.
Correction: The opponents are actually using this statement to try to deny knowledge as an attribute; they are arguing that if selective action implies attribute, then knowledge would also be implied, but they want to reject that implication.

Checkpoint
What do the opponents use to distinguish between knowledge and speech?
A) God's justice and mercy
B) The fact that God taught Moses and Pharaoh but spoke only to Moses
C) The fact that God created all things
D) The fact that God is one

Key Takeaway
The opponents use the example of Moses and Pharaoh to argue that God's speech is an attribute but His knowledge is not, based on the selectivity of speaking versus teaching.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God speaks only to those He chooses, do you actively seek to hear His speech through the Qur'an? Or do you treat it as just general teaching applicable to all?
?
The opponents used selectivity to argue that speech is a special attribute of God. Do you revere the Qur'an as such a unique attribute, or do you approach it as one among many divine actions?
?
When you read the Qur'an, do you feel that God is speaking directly to you, or do you see it as ancient wisdom for all? How does that influence your response?

Al-Ash'ari's Refutation: Consistency in Divine Attributes
Al-Ash'ari's refutation using two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's two arguments refuting the distinction between knowledge and speech, showing that consistency requires affirming both as attributes.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari uses the opponents' own logic: if selective speech implies an attribute, then teaching both implies a knowledge attribute.

After presenting the opponents' view, al-Ash'ari responds with two powerful arguments. He first points out the inconsistency in their reasoning: if they accept that God's selective speaking implies a speech attribute, then His teaching both individuals should imply a knowledge attribute covering both. Then he uses the example of God's speech to all things by 'Be' to show that knowledge of all things is analogous.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Ash'ari's first argument
Read the first argument carefully. It turns the opponents' own reasoning against them.
The key is the parallel between speaking and teaching.
2
Understand the parallel
Al-Ash'ari argues: if speaking to Moses alone implies an attribute of speech, then teaching both Moses and Pharaoh implies an attribute of knowledge that encompasses both.
This shows inconsistency in the opponents' position.
3
Read the second argument
Now read the second argument about God speaking to things by 'Be'.
This argument uses universal speech to argue for universal knowledge.
4
Reflect on the force of the refutation
Consider how al-Ash'ari's two arguments together undermine the opponent's distinction.

Source Text

Al-Ash'ari demonstrates that consistency demands affirming knowledge as a divine attribute just as speech is affirmed, using the parallel between speaking and teaching.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ash'ari insists on consistency in affirming God's attributes. Where in your own life are you inconsistent—accepting some truths about God easily while resisting others that challenge your comfort? What attribute of God do you find hardest to truly internalize, and why?
?
If God's teaching both Moses and Pharaoh reveals His comprehensive knowledge, how does that confront any secret belief you might hold that God is unaware of certain details of your life? Will you today ask Him to teach you something you have been avoiding?
?
Al-Ash'ari uses logic to force the opponent to be consistent. Is your belief in God's attributes just intellectual assent, or does it shape how you live? Which attribute—if you truly believed it—would change one decision you have to make this week?

Reductio: From Speech Specificity to Power
Al-Ash'ari uses an analogy to refute denial of knowledge by specificity of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari employs a reductio ad absurdum to expose the inconsistency in the opponents' reasoning about divine attributes.

Core Idea
If speech is more specific than knowledge, then power must also be an attribute.

In this passage, al-Ash'ari turns the opponents' own logic against them. They claimed God has speech but not knowledge because speech is more specific. Using the same principle, he argues they must also affirm that God has power, since knowledge is more general than power. The example of the Qadariyya shows that they already consider power more specific, so consistency demands they accept power as an attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage aloud or silently. Notice the structure: 'then it is said to them' introduces the reductio.
The passage uses logical terms like 'أخص' (more specific) and 'أعم' (more general).
2
Identify the opponent's premise
The opponents affirm speech but deny knowledge because speech is more specific. Understand this premise.
Al-Ash'ari is responding to a specific sectarian view.
3
Follow the reductio argument
Al-Ash'ari applies the same logic: if specificity excludes the more general attribute, then power should also be affirmed because knowledge is more general than power according to them.
4
Examine the Qadariyya example
The Qadariyya sect holds that God can create disbelief, meaning they affirm power as a specific attribute. Al-Ash'ari uses this to show inconsistency.
The opponents are being forced to accept power or abandon their own reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: إذا أوجبتم أن الله كلاماً وليس له علم؛ لأن الكلام أخص من العلم والعلم أعم منه، فقولوا : إن الله قدرة؛ لأن العلم أعـم عنـدكـم مـن القدرة ؛ لأن من مذاهب القدرية أنهم لا يقولون إن الله لا يقدر أن يخلق الكفر، فقد أثبتوا القدرة أخص من العلم، فينبغي لهم أن يقولوا على اعتلالهم إن الله قدرة
Then it is said to them: If you affirm that God has speech but not knowledge, because speech is more specific than knowledge and knowledge is more general than it, then say that God has power, because knowledge according to you is more general than power; because from the doctrines of the Qadariyya is that they say that God is able to create disbelief, so they affirm that power is more specific than knowledge, so they must say according to their reasoning that God has power.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a 'جواب' (response) using a reductio ad absurdum. The opponents claim that God has speech but not knowledge because speech is more specific (أخص) than knowledge. Al-Ash'ari retorts: If that reasoning holds, then you should also say that God has power (قدرة), because knowledge is more general than power. The Qadariyya (a sect) hold that God is able to create disbelief, meaning they affirm power as a specific attribute. But they deny that God has power in the sense of creating disbelief? Actually the text: 'because from the doctrines of the Qadariyya is that they do not say that God is not able to create disbelief' — i.e., the Qadariyya affirm that God can create disbelief, so they consider power to be more specific than knowledge. Therefore, by their own logic, they should affirm that God has power (as an attribute). This shows the inconsistency in their reasoning.

Key Terms
أخص (more specific)
أعم (more general)
قدرة (power)
كلام (speech)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari is endorsing the Qadariyya view on power.
Correction: He is using their own doctrine as an example to show inconsistency; he does not agree with them.

Checkpoint
What does al-Ash'ari insist the opponents should affirm based on their own reasoning about specificity?
A) That God has knowledge
B) That God has speech
C) That God has power
D) That God has no attributes

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari demonstrates that the opponents' reasoning about specificity leads to an absurd conclusion: they must affirm power as an attribute, which undermines their denial of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical argument that challenges your current beliefs, do you honestly examine the consistency of your own reasoning, or do you dismiss it? Where in your understanding of Allah's attributes might you be selectively applying principles without realizing it? Will you commit to following the logic wherever it leads, even if it forces you to accept something uncomfortable?
?
The Qadariyya held a specific view about divine power. Is there any belief you hold about God that, if applied consistently, would force you to affirm something you currently deny? What would it take for you to accept a reductio argument like this one?
?
Al-Ash'ari uses the opponents' own principle to refute them. Are you willing to have your own principles turned against you in the pursuit of truth? How would you respond if someone showed that your reasoning leads to a conclusion you reject—would you abandon the principle or the conclusion?

The Generality of 'Knowing' Does Not Negate Speech's Specificity
Al-Ash'ari refutes the claim that speech's specificity negates knowledge by comparing generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari uses the logical relationship between generality and specificity to defend the coexistence of divine attributes.

Core Idea
The 'more general' does not invalidate the 'more specific' — both can be true together.

Al-Ash'ari continues his rebuttal of the Mu'tazili argument that attributes like speech would imply multiplicity. He now uses a logical tool: the relationship between a more general attribute (knowing) and a more specific one (speaker). By forcing the opponent to apply their own reasoning consistently, he shows that their argument leads to absurdity. Let's read this concise but powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation. Let the argument's flow settle in your mind.
Notice the question-and-challenge structure.
2
Identify the key terms
Locate the terms for 'knowing' (عالم), 'speaker' (متكلم), 'addressed' (مكلَّم), and 'more general' (أعم). Understand their roles.
3
Trace the logical move
The opponent argues that because speech is more specific, it negates knowledge. Al-Ash'ari turns this around: knowing is more general, so why doesn't that negate speech? The point is that specificity does not negate generality.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this argument allows both attributes to coexist. The opponent's reasoning is shown to be inconsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال: أليس الله عالماً، والوصف له بأنه عالم أعم من الوصف له بأنه متكلم مكلَّم؟ ثم لم يجب؛ لأن الكلام أخص من أن يكون الله تعالى متكلماً غير عالم؟ فلم لا قلتم إن الكلام - وإن كان أخص من العلم - لا ينفي أن يكون الله علم، كما لم ينف بخصوص الكلام أن يكون الله عالماً
Then it is said: Is God not knowing? And the description of Him as knowing is more general than the description of Him as a speaker addressed? Then why is it not necessary? Because speech is more specific than that God be a speaker without being knowing? So why do you not say that speech, even if it is more specific than knowledge, does not negate that God has knowledge, just as the specificity of speech does not negate that God is knowing?
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is another 'جواب' that turns the opponents' argument on its head. The author asks: Is God not knowing (عالم)? The description 'knowing' is more general (أعم) than 'speaker addressed' (متكلم مكلّم). If the opponents argue that because speech is more specific, God has speech but not knowledge, then by the same logic, because knowing is more general, God should be knowing but not speaker? But that is false. Therefore, the specificity of speech does not negate knowledge; likewise, the generality of knowing does not negate speech. The conclusion is that the argument from specificity is invalid—both attributes can coexist.

Key Terms
عالم (knowing)
متكلم (speaker)
مكلَّم (addressed)
أعم (more general)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an attribute is more specific than another, it cannot coexist with the more general attribute.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that specificity does not negate generality; both can be true simultaneously, as in the case of knowing and speaking.

Checkpoint

A) That knowledge is also specific
B) That 'knowing' is more general, yet it does not negate speech
C) That God does not have speech
D) That specificity only applies to creation

Key Takeaway
The specificity of an attribute does not negate the existence of a more general attribute; both can be affirmed of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Proving God's Knowledge: From Scripture and Creation
Al-Ash'ari gives a two-part proof of God's knowledge attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari uses both Quranic verses and the order of creation to prove that God possesses the attribute of knowledge.

Core Idea
Scripture and creation both testify that God's knowledge is an inherent attribute.

Al-Ash'ari presents a two-pronged response to those who deny that God has a distinct attribute of knowledge. He first challenges them to accept the logical implication of the very verses they quote, then points to the wisdom in creation as further evidence. This lesson walks through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Al-Ash'ari quotes the opponents' proof and then turns it against them.
Pay attention to the verse citations.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage contains two distinct proofs: one from scripture and one from creation. Find where each appears.
The second proof begins with the words 'فإن قالوا: قلنا'.
3
Consider the implication
Why does Al-Ash'ari insist that saying 'God is knowing' implies He has knowledge? Reflect on the difference between describing an action and affirming an attribute.
This is the core of the debate over divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم: من أين علمتم أن الله عالم؟ فإن قالوا: بقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّهُ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمٌ ﴾ [الشورى: ۱۲] . قيل لهم : ولذلك فقولوا : إن الله علماً بقوله : أَنزَلَهُ يعلمه ) [النساء: ١٦٦] وبقوله: ﴿وَمَا تَحْمِلُ مِنْ أُنثَى وَلَا تَضَعُ إِلَّا بِعِلْمِهِ) [فاطر:۱۱] وكذلك قولوا: إن له قوة لقوله : ( أَوَلَمْ يَرَوْا أَنَّ اللَّهَ الَّذِى خَلَقَهُمْ هُوَ أَشَدُّ مِنْهُمْ قُوَّةً ) [فصلت : ١٥]. فإن قالوا: قلنا: إن الله عالم ؛ لأنه صنع العالم علـى مـا فـيـه مـن آثـار الحكمة واتساق التدبير
And it is said to them: From where did you know that God is knowing? If they say: By His saying: 'Indeed He is of all things Knowing' [Ash-Shura 12]. It is said to them: Therefore say that God has knowledge by His saying: 'He sent it down with His knowledge' [An-Nisa 166] and by His saying: 'And no female conceives nor gives birth except with His knowledge' [Fatir 11]. And likewise say that He has power by His saying: 'Do they not see that God who created them is more severe than them in strength?' [Fussilat 15]. Then if they say: We say that God is knowing because He created the world with what is in it of signs of wisdom and coherence of arrangement.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a 'جواب' (response) that presents two proofs for God having knowledge as an attribute. The author first challenges the opponents: how do you know God is knowing? If they answer from the Quranic verse 'Indeed He is of all things Knowing' (which describes God as knowing but does not explicitly affirm an attribute of knowledge), the response is to point out that other verses say 'He sent it down with His knowledge' and 'no female conceives... except with His knowledge', which explicitly affirm knowledge (علم) as an entity. The same applies to power (قوة). So the opponents should affirm these attributes from the same scriptural source. If instead the opponents argue from creation (that the world shows wisdom and order), the author implies that this also points to an attribute of knowledge. The passage thus shows that the same evidence used to prove God is knowing also proves He has knowledge as an attribute.

Key Terms
عالم
علم
قوة
آثار الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Quranic verses only describe God's actions without implying intrinsic attributes.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari argues that the same verses used to say 'God is knowing' also indicate He has knowledge as an attribute.

Checkpoint

B) 'Indeed He is of all things Knowing'
C) 'He sent it down with His knowledge'
D) 'Do they not see that God is stronger?'

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari uses both revealed text and observed creation to affirm that knowledge is a real attribute of God.

Consistency in Divine Attributes: Speech and Power
Al-Ash'ari exposes inconsistency in opponents' reasoning about divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari uses logical consistency to refute those who affirm speech but deny knowledge of Allah.

Core Idea
Consistency demands that if speech is specific, so is power.

Al-Ash'ari presents a sharp logical argument against opponents who accept that Allah has speech but reject that He has knowledge. He shows that the very reasoning they use would force them to accept that Allah is power, which they do not. This exposes the inconsistency in their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the logical flow. Notice the structure: opponent's claim, analogy, reference to Qadariyyah, conclusion.
2
Identify the opponent's premise
The opponent affirms speech (kalam) but denies knowledge (ilm) because speech is more specific (akhass) than knowledge.
What is the reasoning behind this? They think a more specific attribute implies a more general one? Actually they affirm the specific without the general.
3
Al-Ash'ari's counter
Al-Ash'ari says: by the same logic, since knowledge is more general than power (qudra), you should affirm power as a specific attribute. He notes that the Qadariyyah actually hold that Allah cannot create disbelief, which means power is more specific.
This is a reductio: if your reasoning leads to a conclusion you reject, your reasoning is flawed.
4
Understand the reference to Qadariyyah
The Qadariyyah sect denied Allah's power over human actions like disbelief. They held that power is more specific than knowledge because it does not cover all things. Al-Ash'ari uses their own position to show inconsistency.
This strengthens the argument by showing the opponent already implicitly accepts the logic they reject.
5
Conclude the inconsistency
Al-Ash'ari concludes that the opponent must either accept power as an attribute of Allah or abandon their reasoning about speech and knowledge. Their position is self-contradictory.
This is a classic kalam method: use the opponent's own premises to force a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: إذا أوجبتم أن لله كلاماً وليس له علم؛ لأن الكلام أخص من العلم والعلم أعم منه، فقولوا: إن الله قدرة؛ لأن العلم أعندكم من القدرة؛ لأن من مذاهب القدرية أنهم لا يقولون إن الله لا يقدر أن يخلق الكفر، فقد أثبتوا القدرة أخص من العلم، فينبغي لهم أن يقولوا على اعتلالهم إن الله قدرة.
Then it is said to them: If you affirm that Allah has speech but He does not have knowledge, because speech is more specific than knowledge and knowledge is more general than it, then say that Allah has power, because knowledge according to you is more general than power; because from the doctrines of the Qadariyyah is that they do not say that Allah cannot create disbelief, so they have affirmed power as more specific than knowledge. So it is incumbent upon them, according to their reasoning, to say that Allah is power.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a refutation argument against those who claim Allah has speech (kalam) but deny He has knowledge (ilm). Al-Ash'ari points out an inconsistency: if they argue that speech is more specific (akhass) than knowledge, and knowledge is more general (a'amm), then by the same logic, they should also say Allah is power (qudra) because knowledge is more general than power. He notes that the Qadariyyah (a sect) actually affirm that Allah cannot create disbelief, implying that power is more specific than knowledge. Therefore, their own reasoning forces them to attribute power to Allah just as they attribute speech. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if they deny knowledge based on specificity, they must also deny power, but they don't—so their position is inconsistent.

Key Terms
كلام
علم
قدرة
أخص
أعم
القدرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument means that speech and power are identical attributes.
Correction: It does not equate them; it only shows that the opponent's reasoning about specificity is inconsistent.

Checkpoint
What does al-Ash'ari argue the Qadariyyah must say according to their own reasoning about speech and knowledge?
A) Allah is power
B) Allah is not powerful
C) Knowledge is more specific than speech
D) Speech is more general than power

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's argument shows that denying knowledge while affirming speech is logically untenable because it would force one to also affirm power, which the opponents reject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You believe Allah is all-knowing and all-powerful, but do you sometimes act as if His knowledge is limited to what you understand? In what area of your life must you submit to His full knowledge and power?

?

Consistency Between Descriptions of Allah: Speech and Knowledge
Al-Ash'ari questions the inconsistency of denying knowledge while affirming speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari uses the relationship between general and specific attributes to argue for the consistency of Allah's descriptions.

Core Idea
Specificity does not negate generality in divine attributes.

Al-Ash'ari continues his logical argument by examining the attribute of knowledge (ʿilm) in relation to speech (kalām). He points out that 'knowing' is a more general description than 'speaking,' so the specificity of speech should not be used to deny that Allah is knowing. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the rhetorical flow of al-Ash'ari's question sink in.
Pay attention to the terms 'aʿamm' (more general) and 'akhaṣṣ' (more specific).
2
Identify the general and specific attributes
Note that 'ʿālim' (knowing) is described as more general (aʿamm) than 'mutakallim' (speaking). The specific attribute is speech.
3
Reflect on the logical principle
Ask yourself: Why does al-Ash'ari argue that the specificity of speech does not negate the generality of knowledge? Consider the principle that a specific attribute does not exclude a more general one.
This principle is key to understanding the consistency of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال: أليس الله عالماً، والوصف له بأنه عالم أعم من الوصف له بأنه متكلم مكلَّم؟ ثم لم يجب؛ لأن الكلام أخص من أن يكون الله تعالى متكلماً غير عالم؟ فلم لا قلتم إن الكلام - وإن كان أخص من العلم - لا ينفي أن يكون الله عالماً، كما لم ينف بخصوص الكلام أن يكون الله عالماً.

Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage continues the argument by focusing on the attribute 'Alim' (knowing). Al-Ash'ari asks: Isn't the description 'knowing' more general than the description 'speaking'? If so, then the fact that speech is specific should not negate that Allah is knowing. Yet the opponents deny knowledge while affirming speech. Al-Ash'ari points out the contradiction: they accept that Allah is described as knowing (which is more general), so why do they use the specificity of speech to deny knowledge? The argument shows that specificity does not automatically negate the more general attribute.

Al-Ash'ari establishes that the specificity of speech does not negate the more general attribute of knowledge, preserving coherence in divine descriptions.

Proving Divine Attributes Through Revelation
Al-Ash'ari demonstrates that if they prove knowledge by verses, they must also prove power by similar verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari uses scriptural and rational arguments to establish that Allah possesses both knowledge and power.

Core Idea
If revelation proves one attribute, it proves others too.

In this passage, al-Ash'ari engages with opponents who accept that Allah is 'Alim (knowing) based on a verse. He shows that consistency requires them to also affirm Allah's knowledge and power from other verses. He then addresses a second line of reasoning from the design of the world, demonstrating that the same logic yields both attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let al-Ash'ari's argument unfold naturally.
Note that there are two distinct moves in the argument.
2
Identify the two lines of argument
First, al-Ash'ari uses scriptural proof: opponents accept verse 42:12 for knowledge, so he cites verses 4:166 and 35:11 for knowledge and 41:15 for power. Second, he uses the argument from design: the world shows wise crafts, which require a knower and a powerful agent.
The second argument shows that reason alone also demands both attributes.
3
Understand the consistency requirement
Ask yourself: Why does al-Ash'ari insist that if the opponent proves knowledge from scripture, they must also prove power from scripture? The answer lies in the principle that the same method should be applied consistently.
This is a dialectical move to force the opponent to accept all attributes that scripture teaches.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم: من أين علمتم أن الله عالم؟ فإن قالوا: بقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّهُ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمٌ﴾ [الشورى: 12]. قيل لهم: ولذلك فقولوا: إن الله علم بقوله: ﴿أَنزَلَهُ بِعِلْمِهِ﴾ [النساء: 166] وبقوله: ﴿وَمَا تَحْمِلُ مِنْ أُنثَى وَلَا تَضَعُ إِلَّا بِعِلْمِهِ﴾ [فاطر: 11] وكذلك قولوا: إن له قوة لقوله: ﴿أَوَلَمْ يَرَوْا أَنَّ اللَّهَ الَّذِى خَلَقَهُمْ هُوَ أَشَدُّ مِنْهُمْ قُوَّةً﴾ [فصلت: 15]. فإن قالوا: قلنا: إن الله عالم؛ لأنه صنع العالم على ما فيه من آثار الحكمة واتساق التدبير. قيل لهم: فلم لا قلتم إن الله علم بما ظهر في العالم من حكمه وآثار تدبيره؟ لأن الصنائع الحكمية لا تظهر إلا من ذي علم، كما لا تظهر إلا من عالم، وكذلك لا تظهر إلا من ذي قوة كما لا تظهر إلا من قادر.
And it is said to them: From where do you know that Allah is knowing? If they say: By His statement, the Most High: 'Indeed, He is of all things Knowing' (Quran 42:12). It is said to them: Therefore say that Allah has knowledge by His statement: 'He revealed it with His knowledge' (Quran 4:166) and by His statement: 'And no female conceives nor gives birth except with His knowledge' (Quran 35:11). And likewise say that He has power by His statement: 'Have they not seen that Allah, who created them, is greater than them in strength' (Quran 41:15). If they say: We say that Allah is knowing because He made the world with what is in it of traces of wisdom and coherence of governance. It is said to them: Then why do you not say that Allah has knowledge by what has appeared in the world of His wisdom and traces of His governance? For wise crafts do not appear except from one with knowledge, just as they do not appear except from a knower, and likewise they do not appear except from one with power, just as they do not appear except from a powerful one.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument where al-Ash'ari addresses a possible proof from the opponents. They claim to know Allah is 'Alim' through a verse (42:12). Al-Ash'ari then points out that other verses also indicate knowledge and power (4:166, 35:11, 41:15). If they accept scriptural proof for 'Alim,' they must also accept it for 'knowledge' and 'power.' Then al-Ash'ari considers an alternative: maybe they prove Allah's knowledge from the wisdom evident in creation. Al-Ash'ari counters that the same reasoning shows Allah has knowledge and power, because wise effects require a knower and a powerful agent. Thus, the opponent's own method forces them to affirm both attributes.

Key Terms
عالم
علم
قوة
حكمة
تدبير
صنائع حكمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Ash'ari is proving God's existence from design (teleological argument).
Correction: Actually, al-Ash'ari is not proving existence; he is refuting those who deny attributes while using design to prove knowledge. He shows that design proves both knowledge and power.

Checkpoint
What does al-Ash'ari say about wise crafts?
A) They appear only from a knower and a powerful one
B) They appear only from a powerful one but not a knower
C) They appear only from a knower but not a powerful one
D) They appear from anyone

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari shows that both scripture and reason consistently require Allah to have both knowledge and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the order and wisdom in the world, do you honestly stop at acknowledging a Designer, or do you let that recognition lead you to affirm all His attributes, even the ones that demand obedience and accountability from you? ?

?

Names Imply Attributes
Al-Ash'ari rebuts the denial of attributes by appealing to Quranic mention of names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why accepting Allah's names from the Quran necessarily implies accepting the attributes they signify, and how this argument exposes inconsistency in the opponents' position.

Core Idea
If His names are in the Book, so are His attributes.

This passage presents a concise scriptural argument from Imam al-Ash'ari. He challenges those who accept the divine names mentioned in the Quran but deny the corresponding attributes. The argument is simple but decisive—if the names are undeniable because they are in the Book, then the attributes (the meanings of those names) are equally undeniable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the dialogue format: a question, an objection, and a rebuttal.
The argument is compressed; let the author's own rhythm come through.
2
Identify the two groups
One group denies Allah's knowledge (and other attributes). The other group (the same? or a hypothetical?) accepts the names because they are in the Quran. Al-Ash'ari addresses the inconsistency.
The 'they' refers to those who deny attributes but accept names.
3
Understand the logical move
If the names are accepted solely because they are mentioned in the Quran, then the same reasoning applies to attributes like knowledge and power, which are also mentioned. The opponent's acceptance of names commits them to accepting attributes.
This is a scriptural argument, not purely rational.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider that names and attributes are closely linked: a name (e.g., 'Alim) points to an attribute (knowledge). Denying the attribute would undermine the meaning of the name.
The passage does not say names are the same as attributes, but that they imply them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم: إذا نفيتم علم الله فلم لا نفيتم أسماءه؟ فإن قالوا: كيف ننفي أسماءه وقد ذكرها في كتابه؟ قيل لهم: فلا تنفوا العلم والقوة؛ لأنه تبارك وتعالى ذكر ذلك في كتابه العزيز.
And it is said to them: If you deny Allah's knowledge, why do you not deny His names? If they say: How can we deny His names when He has mentioned them in His Book? It is said to them: Then do not deny knowledge and power, because He, blessed and exalted, mentioned that in His Mighty Book.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This is a brief but powerful argument from scripture. Al-Ash'ari points out that the opponents accept the divine names (like 'Alim, Qadir') because they are in the Quran. But they deny the corresponding attributes (knowledge, power). If the names are undeniable because they are in the Book, then the attributes (which are the meanings of those names) are also in the Book and thus undeniable. The argument shows the inconsistency of accepting the name but rejecting the attribute it signifies.

Key Terms
أسماء
علم
قوة
كتاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that names and attributes are identical, so if you accept the name you don't need to posit a separate attribute.
Correction: This passage shows that names and attributes are distinct yet inseparable. The name 'Alim' signifies the attribute of knowledge, but the name is not the same as the attribute. Accepting the name without the attribute is inconsistent.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Ash'ari say the opponents should not deny knowledge and power?
A) Because they are the same as names
B) Because they are mentioned in the Book just like names
C) Because knowledge is more general
D) Because the Prophet taught them

Key Takeaway
Accepting Allah's names from the Quran logically requires accepting the attributes they signify, because both are equally present in divine revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever accept some parts of the Quran (like the beautiful names of Allah) but resist others (like His attributes of power and knowledge) because they demand more from you? Will you commit to accepting every mention of Allah in His Book, even those that confront your comfort?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's knowledge and power are as real as His mercy and love, how would your dua change tonight? Would you plead with more urgency, knowing He hears and is able?
?
The opponents in this passage were inconsistent—they accepted names but denied attributes. Is there an attribute of Allah that you struggle to fully affirm? What would it take for you to surrender to the full reality of His revelation?

Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
Allah's Teaching Proves His Knowledge
Al-Ash'ari argues that Allah's teaching of the Prophet proves His knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari uses the fact that Allah taught the Prophet to prove that Allah possesses knowledge.

Core Idea
Teaching always implies knowledge; Allah taught the Prophet, so He must know.

In this passage, Al-Ash'ari presents a concise logical argument drawn from the Quranic reality that Allah taught the Prophet the laws and rulings. He argues that it is impossible for someone to teach what they do not know, so Allah must have knowledge. The passage concludes with a declaration of Allah's transcendence above the Jahmiyya denial of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the logical flow: premise, principle, conclusion, and refutation.
The argument is simple but foundational.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument begins with the premise that Allah taught the Prophet the laws and rulings. This is an accepted fact for Muslims.
What does teaching require?
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Since teaching requires knowledge, Allah must have known what He taught. Therefore, the Jahmiyya who deny divine knowledge are refuted.
Al-Ash'ari ends by exalting Allah above their false claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم: قد علم الله تعالى نبيه صلى الله عليه وسلم الشرائع والأحكام، والحلال والحرام، ولا يجوز أن يُعَلِّمَهُ ما لا يعلمه، فكذلك لا يجوز أن يُعَلِّم الله نبيه ما لا عِلْمَ الله به تعالى الله عن قول الجهمية علواً كبيراً.
And it is said to them: Allah, the Most High, taught His Prophet, peace be upon him, the laws and rulings, the permissible and the forbidden, and it is not permissible that He teaches him what He does not know. So likewise, it is not permissible that Allah teaches His Prophet what Allah has no knowledge of. Exalted is Allah above the saying of the Jahmiyyah, a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a simple but decisive argument based on the fact that Allah taught the Prophet (ﷺ) religious laws. Teaching necessarily implies that the teacher has knowledge of what is taught. It is impossible for someone to teach what they do not know. Therefore, Allah must have knowledge. Al-Ash'ari ends by declaring Allah exalted above the false claims of the Jahmiyyah (who denied divine attributes).

Key Terms
تعليم
علم
نبيه
شرائع
أحكام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Jahmiyya claim that Allah does not have knowledge (or attributes).
Correction: Al-Ash'ari argues that because Allah taught the Prophet, He must have knowledge, so the Jahmiyya claim is false.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Ash'ari argue that Allah must have knowledge?
A) Because the Prophet taught laws
B) Because Allah taught the Prophet laws
C) Because the Jahmiyyah said so
D) Because the Quran says 'He knows'

Key Takeaway
Allah's teaching of the Prophet proves that He has knowledge, because teaching requires knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn something about your faith, do you truly believe that it comes from the All-Knowing Creator who personally taught His Prophet? How does this conviction affect your commitment to act on that knowledge?
?
If teaching requires knowledge, what does the quality of your own teaching—whether in parenting, friendship, or work—reveal about your own heart? Are you teaching others things you haven't fully learned or lived yourself?

Divine Attributes

Emotional Attributes Necessitate Real Meanings
Al-Ash'ari argues that cursing, anger, and pleasure imply real attributes in Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari uses analogical reasoning to argue that emotional actions of Allah (cursing, anger, pleasure) imply real attributes, just as knowledge implies an attribute of knowledge.

Core Idea
If Allah curses or is angry, these actions require real attributes, not just metaphors.

This passage from Al-Ash'ari's Al-Ibanah presents a key argument against those who deny that Allah has real attributes. He uses a simple analogical method: if an action we attribute to Allah has a corresponding meaning in human actions, then it must be a real attribute in Allah. We'll see how he builds this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial premise
Read the first sentence: Al-Ash'ari asks if the curse of Allah and the curse of the Prophet both have meaning. Notice that the opponents agree.
2
Trace the extension to knowledge
Next, he extends the same logic to knowledge: if Allah teaches the Prophet, both have knowledge. See how this establishes a pattern.
This shows that the principle is general.
3
Apply the principle to other attributes
Finally, he applies the principle to anger, pleasure, life, hearing, and sight. Each requires its corresponding attribute.
This completes the argument for real attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم: أليس إذا لعن الله الكافرين فلعنه لهم معنى، ولعن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لهم معنى؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. فيقال لهم: فما أنكرتم من أن الله تعالى إذا علم نبيه شيئاً فكان للنبي علم، والله سبحانه علم، وإذا كنا متى أثبتناه غاضباً على الكافرين فلا بد من إثبات غضب، وكذلك إذا أثبتناه راضياً عن المؤمنين فلا بد من إثبات رضى، وكذلك إذا أثبتناه حياً سميعاً بصيراً فلا بد من إثبات حياة وسمع وبصر.
And it is said to them: Does it not happen that if Allah curses the disbelievers, then His curse upon them has a meaning, and the curse of the Prophet, peace be upon him, upon them has a meaning? If they say: Yes. Then it is said to them: So what prevents you from [affirming] that if Allah teaches the Prophet something, then the Prophet has knowledge and Allah, subhanahu, has knowledge? And whenever we affirm that He is angry with the disbelievers, it is necessary to affirm anger, and likewise if we affirm that He is pleased with the believers, it is necessary to affirm pleasure, and likewise if we affirm that He is living, hearing, seeing, it is necessary to affirm life, hearing, and sight.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage uses an analogical argument. Al-Ash'ari gets the opponents to agree that Allah's curse on disbelievers has a real meaning, just as the Prophet's curse has a meaning. He then extends this to knowledge: if Allah teaches the Prophet, then both Prophet and Allah have knowledge. He then broadens the principle: whenever we affirm a descriptive attribute of Allah (e.g., angry, pleased, living, hearing, seeing), it necessarily implies the corresponding attribute (anger, pleasure, life, hearing, sight). This establishes that attributes are real and distinct, not just metaphorical.

Key Terms
لعن
غضب
رضى
حياة
سمع
بصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some deny that attributes like anger and pleasure are real in Allah, claiming they are mere metaphors for His actions.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that if we say Allah curses or is angry, we must affirm the corresponding attribute (curse, anger) as a real meaning, just as with knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, what does affirming that Allah is angry with disbelievers require?
A) Denial of anger
B) Affirmation of anger as an attribute
C) Only metaphorical interpretation
D) Affirmation that anger is a creation

Key Takeaway
If you attribute an action to Allah, you must affirm the corresponding attribute as real.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah's anger is real, or have you reduced it to a metaphor for punishment? Examine your heart: when you read verses about His anger, do you feel a genuine fear that changes how you live, or do you explain it away? Commit today to take His anger as a real attribute that demands your repentance.
?
The passage uses the Prophet's curse as an analogy. Do you treat Allah's actions as concrete realities in your daily life? For example, when you hear that Allah hears and sees, do you act as if you are constantly aware of that? This week, pick one attribute (like hearing) and actively remind yourself of its reality in every situation.
?
If Allah's attributes are real, then your relationship with Him must be real. Are you living as if His pleasure or anger truly matters? What would change in your worship if you internalized that Allah's attributes are not abstract concepts but real descriptions of your Lord? Write down one specific change you will make starting today.
